Critique of 'Man' as Example of Implicative Denotation
Critique of an example for implicative denotation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the example of 'man' fails as implicative denotation and reconstruct the syllogistic critique using the condition of clear implication.

Core Idea
Implicative denotation requires necessary conception upon hearing the term, not mere factual association.

In this passage, the commentator critiques a common example used for implicative denotation: that 'man' denotes 'capable of knowledge and writing'. The critique shows that this example violates the condition of necessary implication (luzūm bayyin). By understanding this critique, you will sharpen your grasp of what makes a true implicative denotation in the Islamic logical tradition, a tool essential for precise definition in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example under critique
Find the example: the word 'man' is claimed to imply 'capable of knowledge and writing' as an implicative denotation. Note that this example appears in earlier logical works.
The example is said to be problematic because the implication is not necessary.
2
Understand the condition for implicative denotation
For a denotation to be implicative (dalāla iltizāmiyya), the implied meaning must be necessarily conceived upon understanding the literal meaning. This is called luzūm bayyin (clear implication), especially in the more specific sense (bil-ma'nā al-akhass).
The condition is that the conception of the literal triggers the conception of the implied without reasoning.
3
See why the example fails
One can imagine 'man' as a rational animal without immediately thinking of the capability for knowledge and writing. Therefore, the necessary concomitant is absent, and the example does not fulfill the condition.
The text says: 'one might conceive the meaning of man... and be heedless of his being capable of knowledge and writing.'
4
Reconstruct the syllogism
Follow the commentator's reconstruction: minor premise: the capability is not necessarily conceived from 'man'. Major premise: anything that is not necessarily conceived is not a valid example of implicative denotation. Conclusion: the capability is not a valid example.
This is a first-figure categorical syllogism (Barbara). The major premise is based on the definition of implicative denotation.
5
Identify the key logical term
The condition is summarized by the term luzūm bayyin bil-ma'nā al-akhass (clear implication in the more specific sense). This differentiates genuine implicative denotation from accidental association.
This term appears in the last line of the passage.

إذ لا يلزم من تصور معنى الإنسانِ تصورها على ما لا يخفى.

وصنعة الكتابة .

العطار
.
(قَوْلُهُ : هَكَذَا وَقَعَ فِي كُتُبِ الْقَوْمِ المشار إليهِ التَّمثيل المذكور، وجعله مشبهاً بِهِ وإِنْ كانَ عين المذكورِ هُنَا ؛ لَمَّا أَنَّ الألفاظ أعراض تتشخَصُ بِتَشخُص محالها، فتتعدَّدَ بِهَذا الاعتبارِ ، ودَعوَى قُوَّةِ المشبَّهِ بِهِ في وجه الشبه مُتحقِّقَةٌ هُنَا لَمَّا أَنَّ الصَّادرَ عَنِ القَومِ لأسبقيَّتِهِ مُتقرَّرُ في الأذهانِ فَقَوِيَ بِهَذا الاعتبار. (قَوْلُهُ : وَفِيهِ) أي : التَّمثيلُ المذكور بحث ، وذلكَ البَحْثُ هو عدم مطابقتِهِ لِلمثلِ بِهِ، ثمَّ الظَّاهِرُ أنَّ هذا البحثَ مِنْ قَبيل المعارضة لدعوى ضمنيَّةٍ كَأَنَّ الممثْلَ قالَ : إِنَّ المَثَالَ صالح للمدلول الالتزاميّ ، فَعُورِضَ بِقَوْلِ الشَّارحِ : القابلية المذكورة لا يصلحُ مِثَالاً ... إلخ، وذكر دليل هذه الدعوى مُقتَصِراً على ذِكْرِ الصُّغْرَى، وَطَوَى الكُبْرَى، والنَّتيجه وتقريرُ القِيَاسِ هكذا : القابليَّةُ المذكورة لا يلزمُ مِن تَصوُّرِ معنى الإنسانِ تصوُّرُهَا ، وكُلُّ مَا كانَ كَذَلِكَ ؛ لا يصلحُ مِثَالاً للمدلول الالتزامي، ينتج : القابلية المذكورة لا تَصلُحُ مِثَالاً للمدلول الالتزامي ، أمَّا الصُّغْرَى ؛ فَظاهرةٌ، ولذلك قال : كما لا يخفى، وأمَّا الكُبرى ؛ فَهِيَ مبنيَّةٌ على أنَّ المعتبر هو اللُّزومُ البَيِّنُ بالمعنى الأخص .
Chapter: On the definition of the three denotations and their rulings
Thus it occurred in the books of the people. And in it is a discussion; because the mentioned capability is not suitable as an example for the implicative signified; since the conception of it does not necessarily follow from the conception of the meaning of man, as is not hidden.
al-Dasuqi
(His saying: 'Thus it occurred ... etc.') i.e., the exemplification for implicative denotation occurred in the books of the people like this; i.e., like this example that issued from him, and it is the example of the denotation of man for the capable of knowledge and the craft of writing.
(His saying: 'And in it is a discussion') i.e., in what occurred in the books of the people of the example for implicative denotation by capability of knowledge and craft of writing. (His saying: 'Since it does not follow ... etc.') i.e., and that is because one might conceive the meaning of man—that he is a rational animal—and be heedless of his being capable of knowledge and the craft of writing; while in implicative denotation it is necessary that the conception of the implicative signified be necessary for the conception of the literal signified; in that case, there is no correlation between them, so the exemplification by what was mentioned is not valid.
al-'Attar
(His saying: 'Thus it occurred in the books of the people' refers to the mentioned example, and he made it the object of comparison even though it is the same as what is mentioned here, because words are accidents that become specific by the specificity of their loci, so they multiply by this consideration; and the claim of strength of the object of comparison in the point of similarity is realized here because what issued from the people due to its priority is established in minds, so it became strong by this consideration. (His saying: 'And in it') i.e., the mentioned example has a discussion, and that discussion is its lack of correspondence to the object of comparison. Then it appears that this discussion is of the type of objection to an implicit claim, as if the exemplifier said: 'The example is valid for the implicative signified,' so it was objected by the commentator's saying: 'The mentioned capability is not suitable as an example ... etc.,' and he mentioned the proof of this claim, restricting himself to mentioning the minor premise, and omitted the major premise and the conclusion. The syllogism is set up thus: The mentioned capability does not necessarily follow from the conception of the meaning of man, and everything that is like that is not suitable as an example for the implicative signified; it follows: The mentioned capability is not suitable as an example for the implicative signified. As for the minor premise, it is apparent, and therefore he said: 'as is not hidden.' As for the major premise, it is based on the fact that what is considered is the clear implication in the more specific sense.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of an example used for implicative denotation (dalāla iltizāmiyya). It begins with a section heading indicating that this part treats the definitions and rulings of the three denotations. The author notes that in the books of the logicians, an example occurs: the word 'man' is said to imply 'capable of knowledge and writing'. The critique is that this example is invalid because the capability of knowledge and writing is not necessarily conceived when one understands the meaning of 'man'. One might think of 'man' as a rational animal without immediately thinking of that capability. For implicative denotation to be valid, the implied meaning must necessarily accompany the literal meaning in the mind. Since this is not the case, the example fails. al-Dasuqi comments that the example as presented in those books is flawed. al-'Attar adds further nuance: even if the example appears the same, the context of the utterance can affect its strength; however, the core issue remains that the example does not satisfy the condition of necessary implication. The commentator then reconstructs the argument in syllogistic form: the minor premise is that the capability is not necessarily conceived from 'man' (which is obvious), and the major premise is that only if the implication is clear in the more specific sense (luzūm bayyin bil-ma'nā al-akhass) is it a valid example. Thus, the example is invalid. This highlights a key condition: implicative denotation requires that the implication be self-evident upon understanding the literal term.

Key Terms
دلالة التزامية
لزوم بيّن بالمعنى الأخص

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the example of 'man' denoting 'capable of knowledge and writing' as implicative denotation?
A) Because man is not actually capable of knowledge and writing
B) Because the capability is not part of the definition of man
C) Because the capability is not necessarily conceived upon imagining man
D) Because the capability contradicts the meaning of man

Key Takeaway
Implicative denotation requires that the implied meaning be necessarily conceived upon understanding the literal term; the example of 'man' fails this condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the term 'khamr' (wine) in Islamic law. Does the term 'khamr' imply 'forbidden' (haram) as implicative denotation? Apply the condition of necessary conception: when one hears 'khamr', does the conception of 'khamr' immediately entail the conception of 'haram'? If not, what would be a better example of implicative denotation?
?
The definition of 'ghasb' (usurpation) is 'taking another's property wrongfully.' Evaluate whether the term 'ghasb' implies 'wrongfulness' as an implicative denotation using the luzūm bayyin condition. Justify your answer.

Definition of Evident Implication and Its Two Types
Author defines evident implication and distinguishes its two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between evident implication in the more general sense and the more specific sense, and identify the relationship between them according to the two methods.

Core Idea
Evident implication splits into two types: one where certainty is immediate, another where both terms are needed.

This passage answers an objection by clarifying the type of implication between man and a capability. The author defines evident implication (al-luzum al-bayyin) and divides it into two subtypes. Understanding this distinction is essential for analyzing how logical implications are recognized, a tool used in Islamic legal theory (usul al-fiqh) to derive rulings from texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the broader context
Recognize that the passage responds to an objection about the implication between man and a certain capability. The author asserts it is evident implication in the more general sense.
2
Extract the definition
Evident implication means that when both the implied term (malzum) and the consequent (lazim) are conceived, the mind is certain of the connection.
The definition emphasizes that conceiving only the implied term is insufficient.
3
Differentiate the two types
For the more general (a'amm): conceiving both terms gives certainty, but the certainty does not depend on prior separate conception of the consequent. For the more specific (akhass): both must be conceived for certainty to occur.
The key difference is whether conceiving the implied term alone can lead to certainty (it cannot, but in the more general, the certainty does not depend on the consequent's separate conception).
4
Analyze the relationship between the two types
Two methods exist: one claims mutual exclusion (tabayun), the other claims absolute generality and specificity (umum wa khusus mutlaq). The example of 'man differs from horse' illustrates the more specific type because both man and the difference must be conceived.
The author seems to favor the second method as the summary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويمكن أن يُجابَ عنه: بأنَّ اللُّزوم بين الإنسانِ والقابلية المذكورة هو اللُّزومُ البَيِّن بالمعنى الأعم، وهو: ألا يكون تصور الملزوم فقط كافياً في جزم العقل باللزومِ. وقضيَّةُ كلام الشارح: إِنَّ بَينَ اللَّزومِ البَيِّنِ بالمعنى الأعمّ واللُّزوم البين بالمعنى الأخص تبايناً، وهو طريقة. وهناك طريقةٌ أُخرى وهي: أنَّ اللُّزومَ البيِّنَ بالمعنى الأعم هو الذي إذا تصوَّرَ الملزوم واللازم؛ جزمَ العقلُ باللُّزوم أعم من كون الجزم باللزوم متوقفاً على تصورِ اللازم أم لا، وأنَّ اللُّزومَ البيِّنَ بالمعنى الأخصّ كَمَا ذَكَرَهُ الشَّارح، وعلى هذا؛ قبينَهُما عموم وخصوص مطلق. والحاصلُ: أنَّ اللُّزومَ إِمَّا غَيرُ بين، وإمَّا بيّن، واللزوم البين تحته فردان: لزوم بين بالمعنى الأعم، ولزوم بين بالمعنى الأخص. وذلكَ كَلزوم مُغايرة الإنسان للفرس، فإذا تصوّر الإنسان والمغايرة المذكورة؛ جزمَ العقلُ بلزومِهَا له، ولا يكفي في جزم العقل بلزومها تصوُّرُ الإنسان فقط؛ لجواز الغفلة عن الفرس وعن مغايرة الإنسان لها، فلا يحصل الجزم باللزوم.
And it can be answered: that the implication between man and the mentioned capability is the evident implication in the more general sense, which is: that the conception of the implied term alone is not sufficient for the mind to be certain of the implication. And the implication of the commentator's statement: that there is mutual exclusion between the evident implication in the more general sense and the evident implication in the more specific sense—this is one method. And there is another method: that the evident implication in the more general sense is that when both the implied term and the consequent are conceived, the mind is certain of the implication, regardless of whether the certainty depends on conceiving the consequent or not; and the evident implication in the more specific sense is as the commentator mentioned; and on this, there is absolute generality and specificity between them. The summary: implication is either non-evident or evident, and under evident implication there are two types: evident implication in the more general sense and evident implication in the more specific sense. And that is like the implication of the difference of man from horse: when one conceives man and the mentioned difference, the mind is certain of it applying to him, but conceiving man alone is not sufficient for the mind to be certain of it applying to him, due to the possibility of being heedless of the horse and of man's difference from it, so certainty of the implication does not occur.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of evident implication (al-luzum al-bayyin) and divides it into two types. It begins as a response to an objection concerning the relationship between man and a certain capability. The author asserts that the implication here is evident in the more general sense, meaning that merely conceiving the implied term (man) is insufficient for the mind to necessarily affirm the implication; rather, both the implied term and the consequent must be considered. The commentator then notes two perspectives on the relationship between the two types of evident implication: the first considers them mutually exclusive, while the second holds that the more general type is broader in scope. According to the second method, the more general type requires only that conceiving both terms leads to certainty, whether or not the consequent is separately conceptualized; the more specific type requires that both are necessary for certainty. The passage concludes with a classification: implication is either non-evident or evident, and evident has two subtypes. An example clarifies: the fact that man differs from horse is an evident implication in the more general sense because to know it necessarily, one must conceive both man and the difference; simply thinking of man does not suffice, as one might overlook the horse.

Key Terms
اللزوم
اللزوم البين
اللزوم البين بالمعنى الأعم
اللزوم البين بالمعنى الأخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of evident implication is exemplified by the statement 'Man is different from horse'?
A) Evident implication in the more general sense
B) Evident implication in the more specific sense
C) Non-evident implication
D) Both types equally

Key Takeaway
Evident implication is divided into two types: the more general (where conceiving both terms yields certainty, but the certainty may or may not depend on the consequent) and the more specific (where conceiving both is necessary for certainty).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the usuli principle that a command (amr) implies obligation (wujub) unless there is a contrary indication. Determine whether this implication is an evident implication in the more general or more specific sense, and justify your answer using the definitions from the passage.

The Condition for Necessary Indication: Both Concepts Required
Definition and refinement of the condition for necessary indication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for necessary indication and explain why both the malzum and the lazim must be conceived for certainty.

Core Idea
Certainty of a necessary relation requires conceiving both relata.

This lesson examines the definition of necessary indication (al-dalalah al-luzumiyyah) and the key condition that the mind must conceive both the necessitated (malzum) and the necessary concomitant (lazim) to achieve certainty. The commentator al-Dasuqi refines the phrasing to explicitly exclude the sufficiency of only the malzum. The example of the difference between 'insan' and 'faras' illustrates why both are needed, as neglecting one leads to heedlessness. Understanding this condition is foundational for sound inferential reasoning in Islamic sciences, ensuring that necessary indications are not mistakenly assumed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Initial Definition
Locate the statement: 'bal la budda fihi min tasawwurihima' (rather it is necessary to conceive both). This sets the condition that both the malzum and lazim must be conceived for the mind to judge the necessity.
The term 'luzum' (necessity) is the relation between them.
2
Understand al-Dasuqi's Refinement
Al-Dasuqi suggests that the definition should explicitly say: 'That which the conceptualization of the malzum alone is not sufficient...' This clarifies that the condition is not just positive but also negative: malzum alone is insufficient.
The refinement makes the definition more precise and concise.
3
Work Through the Example
Consider the necessity of the difference between 'insan' and 'faras'. If you conceive only 'insan', you might be heedless of 'faras' and the difference, so certainty does not arise. Only when you conceive both 'insan' and the difference (lazim) does certainty occur.
The example shows that heedlessness (ghaflah) prevents certainty when one term is omitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في تعريف الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها
بَيْنَ اللازم والملزوم، بل لا بُدَّ فيه من تصورِهما حتَّى يحصل جزم العقل باللزوم بينهما .
الدسوقي
ظاهرُهُ، وَلَوْ قالَ: مَا لا يكون تصوُّرُ الملزوم كافياً في جزمِ العقل به، بل لا بد . . . إلخ؛ كان أظهر وأخصر
.

(قَوْلُهُ : بَلْ لَا بُدَّ ... إِلَخ) وذلكَ كَلزوم مُغايرة الإنسان للفرس، فإذا تصوّر الإنسان والمغايرة المذكورة؛ جزمَ العقلُ بلزومِهَا له، ولا يكفي في جزم العقل بلزومها تصوُّرُ الإنسان فقط؛ لجواز الغفلة عن الفرس وعن مغايرة الإنسان لها، فلا يحصل الجزم باللزوم.
العطار
أَنَّ
(قَوْلُهُ: بَلْ لَا بُدَّ فِيْهِ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِهِمَا أَبَى بِهذا الإضرابِ لِكَوْنِ مَفهومِ اللُّزومِ ثبوتيًّا على مَا فَسَّرَهُ بِهِ القومُ ، ومَفهومُ قَوْلِهِ : قبلهُ ؛ هو أنْ لَا يكونَ تَصوُّرُ الملزوم فقط سلبيًا؛ مُخالف لتعبيراتهم، فالمفهومات لم تَختَلِفْ إِلَّا بِهذهِ الحيثية، وقولُ مَنَ قالَ : لم يكتفِ عن الإضرابِ بِمَا قبلَهُ لاحتمالِ أنْ يكونَ لَا بُدَّ مِن تَصوُّرِ الملزوم واللازم أو الملزوم فقط ; لا يصح، لأنَّ الاحتمالَ الثَّانِي مَنفِيٌّ معَ قيدِهِ، فتعيَّنَ المثبتَ الأَوَّلَ؛ وهو عين الإضراب، فإنْ قُلتَ: اللازم البيِّنُ بالمعنى الأخص هو أن يكونَ تَصوُّرُ الملزومِ واللازم كافياً في جَزْمِ العقلِ باللُّزومِ، وهَذَانِ المفهومانِ متباينان، لا أنَّ أحدَهُما أعمُّ والآخَرَ أخصُّ، وأجابَ المحشّيُّ بأنَّ معنى كَونِهِ أعم ; أَنَّه كُلَّمَا كَانَ تَصوُّرُ الملزوم كافياً ; كانَ تصور الملزومِ واللَّازِمِ كافيَيْنِ، والمراد بكونه كافياً ; عدم الاحتياج إلى وسط، وهو المقترنُ بِلأَنَّهُ في قولنا مثلاً : العالَم حادثُ لأَنَّهُ مُتغيَّرُ ا.هـ. ، ومُحصّل جوابِهِ أَن دَعوَى العُمومِ والخصوص باعتبارِ التَّحقُّقِ; لا باعتبار المفهوم، فالمهومان متباينان، وهذا عكس ما هو الشَّائِعُ مِن أَنَّ النَّسَبَ في المفردات باعتبار المفهوم وفي القضايا باعتبار التحقق، وأجابَ البعضُ بأنَّ مَعنى قولِ الشَّارح : بلْ لَا بُدَّ فيهِ مِن تصورِهِما ; أي: سواءٌ كَانَ تَصوُّرُ الملزوم هو الذي جرَّ إلى تصورِ اللازم أو تَصوُّرِ لا بهِ قالَ: وهذا وجه عُمومه ا.هـ.
Section: On the definition of the three indications and their rulings.
Between the necessary (lazim) and the necessitated (malzum), rather it is necessary in it to conceive both so that the mind's certainty regarding the necessity between them is obtained.
Al-Dasuqi: Its apparent meaning, and if he had said: 'That which the conceptualization of the necessitated alone is not sufficient for the mind's certainty of it, rather both are necessary, etc.' it would be more apparent and more concise.
(His statement: 'between') is an adverb for his statement: 'the necessity.'
(His statement: 'rather it is necessary... etc.) And that is like the necessity of the difference between 'insan' (human) and 'faras' (horse). For if you conceive 'insan' and the mentioned difference, the mind is certain of its necessity for it, and the conceptualization of 'insan' alone is not sufficient for the mind's certainty of its necessity, because of possibility of heedlessness concerning the horse and the difference of 'insan' from it, so certainty of the necessity is not obtained.
Al-'Attar: (His statement: 'Rather it is necessary in it to conceive both') he came with this turning away because the concept of necessity is positive (thubuti) according to what the people (logicians) interpreted it with, and the concept of his statement before it is that the conceptualization of the necessitated alone is not negative, contrary to their expressions. The concepts do not differ except by this aspect. And the statement of whoever said: 'He did not dispense with the turning away by what is before it because of the possibility that it is necessary to conceive the necessitated and the necessary, or the necessitated only' is not valid, because the second probability is negated by its restriction, so the first positive one is determined, and that is the very turning away. If you say: The self-evident necessary in the more specific sense (al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-akhass) is that the conceptualization of the necessitated and the necessary is sufficient for the mind's certainty of the necessity, and these two concepts are mutually exclusive, not that one is general and the other more specific. And the commentator (al-muhashshi) answered that the meaning of its being general is that whenever the conceptualization of the necessitated is sufficient, then the conceptualization of the necessitated and the necessary are both sufficient. And the meaning of 'sufficient' is the lack of need for a middle term, and it is connected with 'because' (li-anna) in our statement, for example: 'The world is originated (hadith) because it is changing.' End quote. And the gist of his answer is that the claim of generality and particularity is with respect to actualization (tahaqquq), not with respect to concept, so the two concepts are mutually exclusive. And this is the opposite of the common notion that the relation in simple terms is with respect to concept, and in propositions with respect to actualization. And some answered that the meaning of the commentator's statement: 'Rather it is necessary in it to conceive both' is: whether the conceptualization of the necessitated is what leads to the conceptualization of the necessary or not. They said: And this is the aspect of its generality. End quote.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical discussion of necessary indication (dalalah luzumiyyah). The author begins by stating the definition: to obtain certainty of a necessary relation, one must conceive both the necessary (lazim) and the necessitated (malzum). Al-Dasuqi then offers a refinement, suggesting that the definition should explicitly state that the mere conception of the malzum is insufficient. Next, the commentator explains the example of the necessity of the difference between 'insan' (human) and 'faras' (horse): if you conceive only 'insan', you may not realize that 'insan' is different from 'faras', but if you conceive both, certainty arises. Al-'Attar then provides a deeper analysis of the phrase 'bal la budda fihi min tasawwurihima'. He explains that the 'idrab' (turning away) is necessary because the concept of 'luzum' is positive (thubuti); the earlier part of the definition implied a negative formulation. He refutes an objection that the 'idrab' could have been avoided, arguing that the second possibility (only malzum) is negated. He then discusses the distinction between the self-evident in the more specific sense (al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-akhass) and the more general sense, using the example 'The world is originated because it is changing' to illustrate that a middle term is needed in non-self-evident cases. The commentators clarify that the claim of generality is about actualization (tahaqquq) not concept, and they conclude by presenting an alternative understanding of the definition.

الملزوم
اللزوم
الإضراب

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it insufficient to conceive only the malzum (e.g., 'insan') for certainty of a necessary relation like the difference from 'faras'?
A) Because the lazim may be unknown
B) Because of possible heedlessness regarding the other concept
C) Because necessity is not a relation
D) Because the malzum alone is self-evident

Key Takeaway
Certainty in necessary indication requires actively conceiving both the necessitated and the necessary concomitant; mere conception of one is prone to heedlessness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the statement 'Every intoxicant is forbidden', explain why, according to this lesson, one must conceive both 'intoxicant' and 'forbidden' to be certain of the necessary relation. Provide an example where conceiving only 'intoxicant' could lead to uncertainty (e.g., someone unaware of the prohibition).
?

Al-'Attar's Refinement: The Two Types of Self-Evident Necessity
Commentary on the idrab and distinction of self-evident necessity types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of self-evident necessity (al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-akhass and al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-a'mm) and explain why their relation is by actualization rather than concept.

Core Idea
The specific self-evident is a subspecies of the general; the difference lies in actual occurrence, not conceptual content.

This lesson delves into al-'Attar's detailed commentary on the phrase 'bal la budda fihi min tasawwurihima'. He argues that the 'idrab' (turning away) is necessary because the concept of luzum is positive (thubuti), and he refutes an objection that it could have been avoided. Then he discusses the distinction between the two types of self-evident necessity: the more specific (al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-akhass) and the general. Using the example 'The world is originated because it is changing', the commentators clarify that the relation between these two is by actualization (tahaqquq) not by concept (mafhum), which is a subtle but crucial point for understanding necessity in Islamic logic and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Idrab
Al-'Attar explains that the shift (idrab) from the earlier part to 'bal la budda...' is necessary because the concept of luzum is positive (thubuti). The earlier wording implied a negative formulation, which is inconsistent with the logicians' conventions.
The idrab corrects the conceptual orientation.
2
Analyse the Refutation of the Objection
Someone objected that the idrab could have been avoided because the earlier part already implies that both are needed. Al-'Attar refutes this by showing that without the idrab, a second probability (only malzum) remains possible, so the idrab is needed to fix the meaning.
The objection is invalid because the earlier part is ambiguous.
3
Identify the Two Types of Self-Evident
The more specific self-evident (al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-akhass) is defined as: conceptualization of both malzum and lazim is sufficient for certainty (no middle term needed). The general self-evident includes cases where a middle term is needed, as in the example 'The world is originated because it is changing'.
The middle term (change) is required in the general case.
4
Grasp the Distinction: Actualization vs. Concept
The commentators state that the relation between the two types is by actualization (tahaqquq), not by concept (mafhum). This means that whenever the specific type occurs, the general also occurs, but the concepts are mutually exclusive (mutabayinan). The specific is a subset in actuality, not in definition.
This reverses the common notion that relations in simple terms are by concept.

فَصْلٌ: في تعريف الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها
بَيْنَ اللازم والملزوم، بل لا بُدَّ فيه من تصورِهما حتَّى يحصل جزم العقل باللزوم بينهما .
الدسوقي
ظاهرُهُ، وَلَوْ قالَ: مَا لا يكون تصوُّرُ الملزوم كافياً في جزمِ العقل به، بل لا بد . . . إلخ؛ كان أظهر وأخصر
.

Two Levels of Self-Evident Necessity: Al-Akhass vs. Al-A'am
Author illustrates two types of self-evident necessity using human capacities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between self-evident necessity in the more specific and more general senses, and identify which type applies to a given necessity relation based on the definition of the subject.

Core Idea
Some necessities follow directly from a definition; others require considering both concepts together.

In Islamic logic, not all self-evident truths are the same. The author explains that the necessity between 'insan' and its capacity for knowledge is of a different order than the necessity between 'insan' and its capacity for writing. This lesson unpacks the distinction between al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-akhass and al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-a'am, using this concrete example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two relations being examined
The author considers the relation between 'insan' (human) and its capacity for knowledge, and between 'insan' and its capacity for writing. Write down these two pairs.
2
Examine the definition of 'insan'
The definition of 'insan' is 'rational animal'. 'Rational' means having the potential to think. Note how knowledge is analytically related to rationality.
3
Apply the test for self-evidence in the more specific sense
Ask: Does the mind immediately accept the necessity upon conceptualizing 'insan' alone? For knowledge, yes; for writing, no. That shows the first relation is bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-akhass.
4
Understand the more general sense
For writing, the mind needs to also conceptualize the specific movement of writing. So the necessity is self-evident only after both concepts are combined. This is bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-a'am.
5
Formulate the rule
A self-evident necessity in the more specific sense is one where the predicate (lazim) is directly derivable from the subject's definition alone. In the more general sense, both subject and predicate need to be considered, but no external proof is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللُّزوم بهذا المعنى بين المعنى الموضوع له وبين القابلية المذكورة ظاهرُ لا مِريَةَ فيه، فإنَّ العقل بعد تصوُّرِ الإنسان والقابلية المذكورة لا يتوقف في اللُّزوم بينهما
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : بهَذَا الْمَعْنَى) أي : المتلبس بهذا المعنى، وهو البين بالمعنى الأعم. (قَوْلُهُ: الْمَعْنَى الْمَوْضُوع لَهُ ) أي : الَّذي وضع له لفظ إنسان، وهو الحيوان الناطق . (قَوْلُهُ : الْقَابِلِيَّةِ الْمَذْكُورَةِ أي : قابليَّةِ الإنسان للكتابة والعِلْم، وقوله : (لا مرية فيه ; أي لا خفاءَ فيه، تأكيد لقوله : (ظاهر). (قَوْلُهُ : ظَاهِرٌ) خبرٌ عن قوله : (واللزوم)، وحيث كان ظاهراً لا خفاء فيه ; فيكونُ قولُهُ: (فإنَّ العقل . . . إلخ)؛ تنبيهاً لا دليلاً . فإنْ قُلْتَ : إِنَّهُ لا ينبّه إلا على ما كانَ فيهِ نوع خفاء، وقد نفاه بقوله : ظاهر،
والجواب : أنَّ المراد بقوله : (ظاهر)؛ يعني : ظهوراً غير تامّ؛ فَلِذَا نَبَّه عليه . (قَوْلُهُ : لَا يَتَوَقَّفُ فِي اللُّزُوْمِ بَيْنَهُمَا ) أي : بل يجزم العقل باللُّزوم بينهما، هذا وما اقتضاه كلامه (۱) من أنَّ اللُّومَ الكائنَ بينَ كلّ من قَبُولِ العِلْم والكتابةِ وبينَ الإنسانِ؛ ليس بيّناً بالمعنى الأخص , بل بالمعنى الأعم , فهو مُسلَّم بالنِّسبةِ لقَبُولِ الكتابة; لا بالنسبةِ لقَبُولِ العِلْمِ; إذْ هو لازم بين بالمعنى الأخص . وذلك لأنَّ الإنسانَ مَعناه حيوان ناطق، والناطق معناه المتفكِّرُ بالقوَّة، فإذا لاحظت الإنسانَ بهذا المعنى ; جزمَ العقل بلزوم قبولِهِ لِلعِلم لزوماً بيّناً بالمعنى الأخصّ، وإذا لاحظت الإنسانَ بهذا المعنى ; فلا يجزمُ العقلُ بلزوم قبولِ الكتابةِ له , بلْ لَا بُدَّ مِن تصورِكَ زيادة على ذلك الكتابة بأنَّها الحركة المخصوصة المبنية على التَّأمل والرَّوِيَّة .
العطار وقد علم أنّ البيِّنَ هو ما لا يفتقر إلى وسط وغير البيِّنِ مَا افتقر إليهِ; سواءٌ كَانَ الوسط واحداً أو مُتعدّداً، كما في الكنايات الكثيرة الوسائط.
(۱) (قَوْلُهُ : وما اقتضاه كلامه ... إلخ) فيه أن الشارح لم يجعل كل واحد منهما لازماً بالمعنى الأعم حتى يردّ عليه بهذا بل مجموع الاثنين هو اللازم البين بالمعنى الأعم بدليل قوله :
بل لا بُدَّ من تصورهما ....هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
And the necessity in this sense between the designated meaning and the mentioned capacity is apparent, no doubt about it. For the mind, after conceptualizing 'insan' and the mentioned capacity, does not hesitate in the necessity between them.
Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'in this sense') i.e., qualified by this sense, and it is the self-evident in the more general sense. (His statement: 'the designated meaning') i.e., that which the term 'insan' was coined for, which is the rational animal. (His statement: 'the mentioned capacity') i.e., the capacity of 'insan' for writing and knowledge. And his statement: 'no doubt about it' means no concealment in it, an emphasis on his statement: 'apparent'. (His statement: 'apparent') is a predicate for his statement: 'the necessity', and since it is apparent with no concealment, then his statement: 'for the mind... etc.' is a notification (tanbih) not a proof. If you say: He only notifies about something that has some concealment, and he has negated that with his statement 'apparent', the answer is that what is meant by his statement 'apparent' is incomplete appearance; therefore he notified about it. (His statement: 'does not hesitate in the necessity between them') i.e., rather the mind is certain of the necessity between them. This and what his statement entails (1) that the necessity existing between each of the acceptance of knowledge and writing and between 'insan' is not self-evident in the more specific sense, but in the more general sense. It is conceded with respect to the acceptance of writing, not with respect to the acceptance of knowledge, for it is a necessary concomitant self-evident in the more specific sense. That is because the meaning of 'insan' is a rational animal, and the meaning of 'rational' is that which has the potential to think. So if you consider 'insan' in this sense, the mind is certain of the necessity of its accepting knowledge in a self-evident manner in the more specific sense. And if you consider 'insan' in this sense, the mind is not certain of the necessity of its accepting writing, rather you must additionally conceptualize writing as the specific movement based on reflection and deliberation.
Al-'Attar: It is known that the self-evident (bayyin) is that which does not need a middle term, and the non-self-evident is that which needs one, whether the middle term is one or multiple, as in indirect expressions (kinayat) with many intermediaries.
(1) (His statement: 'And what his statement entails... etc.) There is in it that the commentator (sharh) did not make each one of them a necessary concomitant in the more general sense so that he could be refuted with this. Rather, the sum of the two is the necessary concomitant self-evident in the more general sense, as evidenced by his statement: 'Rather it is necessary to conceive both.' End quote by al-Shirnubi.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage discusses the distinction between two types of self-evident necessity: al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-akhass (more specific) and al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-a'am (more general). The base text claims that the necessity between the meaning of 'insan' (rational animal) and its capacity for knowledge and writing is apparent. Al-Dasuqi clarifies that this is in the general sense of self-evident. He then explains that the 'apparent' here means incomplete appearance, hence the need for notification (tanbih) rather than a proof. The key distinction emerges: the necessity of 'insan' to accept knowledge is self-evident in the more specific sense, because from the definition of 'insan' as rational (having potential for thought) it directly follows that knowledge is accepted. However, the necessity of 'insan' to accept writing is only self-evident in the more general sense, because writing requires additional conceptualization (deliberate movement). In other words, the capacity for writing is not analytically contained in the concept of 'insan'. Al-'Attar then provides the general rule: a self-evident relation needs no middle term; a non-self-evident one needs one or more intermediaries, like indirect expressions (kinayat). The footnote from al-Shirnubi refines an earlier commentator's point, noting that the sharh did not make each capacity individually a concomitant in the general sense, but rather the sum of the two capacities is the self-evident consequence in the general sense.

Key Terms
البين بالمعنى الأخص
البين بالمعنى الأعم
قابلية
الوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the necessity of a human's capacity for knowledge considered self-evident in the more specific sense (bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-akhass)?
A) Because it is known by all people without reflection
B) Because it follows directly from the definition of 'insan' as a rational animal
C) Because it requires a middle term from logic
D) Because it is a revealed truth from scripture

Key Takeaway
A self-evident necessity in the more specific sense is one where the necessary property is directly derivable from the definition of the subject, while in the more general sense the mind needs to consider both terms together, but no external proof is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the relation between 'khamr' (wine) and its prohibition. The definition of khamr is 'intoxicating beverage'. Does the prohibition follow directly from this definition, or does it require additional concepts? Classify the necessity as bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-akhass or al-a'am, and explain your reasoning based on the example in this lesson.
?
Take another example: the relation between 'human' and 'ability to speak'. Is this necessity self-evident in the specific or general sense? Justify using the definition of 'insan' as rational animal.

The Middle Term and Notification in Classifying Necessities
Commentators elaborate on the rule of the middle term and the role of notification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that self-evident necessities require no middle term, and trace how commentators refine the understanding of a passage through notification and clarification.

Core Idea
The presence or absence of a middle term determines whether a necessity is self-evident or not.

Al-'Attar presents a key rule: the self-evident (bayyin) is that which does not need a middle term (wasat), while the non-self-evident does. This lesson zooms in on this rule and on the commentators' discussion about the nature of 'notification' (tanbih) versus 'proof'. You will see how Al-Dasuqi and Al-Shirnubi refine the base text's claim about the capacities.

Guided Path
1
Identify Al-'Attar's rule
Locate in the passage: 'the self-evident is that which does not need a middle term, and the non-self-evident is that which needs one.' Understand that 'middle term' here means any additional concept needed to establish the necessity.
2
Relate the rule to the example
For the capacity for knowledge, no middle term is needed beyond the definition of insan. For writing, the additional concept of 'writing as a special movement' serves as a middle term. So the knowledge necessity is self-evident; writing necessity is not (in the specific sense).
3
Analyze the commentators' discussion of 'notification' (tanbih)
Al-Dasuqi says the base text's statement that the necessity is 'apparent' is actually incomplete appearance, and therefore the author gives a notification, not a proof. Understand that notification is used when something is clear but still requires a hint to avoid oversight.
4
Examine Al-Shirnubi's refinement
Al-Shirnubi points out that the commentator (Dasuqi) did not treat each capacity individually but considered the sum of both capacities as the necessary concomitant. This shows the layered refinement in the tradition.
5
Apply the rule to a new case
Think of a non-self-evident necessity in Islamic sciences, such as the necessity of prayer for a Muslim. Is a middle term needed? What would it be?

الدسوقي

Distinguishing General vs. Specific Self-Evidence in Logical Implication
Commentators refine the sharih's claim about implication being apparent, distinguishing between specific and general senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'mm and bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass, and apply this distinction to analyze implications in definitions.

Core Idea
Not all self-evident implications are immediate; some require a middle term.

In this passage, the commentators refine the sharih's claim that the implication between 'human' and its potentialities is apparent. They introduce the crucial distinction between two levels of self-evidence: the general and the specific. Understanding this distinction is essential for precise logical analysis, especially in defining terms and establishing entailments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of bayyin
Read al-Dusuqi's explanation: 'bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'mm' (self-evident in the general sense) and 'bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass' (self-evident in the specific sense). Notice that the general sense requires some middle term, while the specific sense does not.
The text says the implication is 'zahir' (apparent) and clarifies it as bayyin in the general sense.
2
Extract the definitions from the text
From al-'Attar's statement: 'al-bayyin huwa ma la yaftaqir ila wasat' (the self-evident is that which does not need a middle term). This defines the specific sense. The general sense allows for a middle term but remains clear after reflection.
The non-self-evident (ghayr bayyin) requires a middle term, whether single or multiple.
3
Apply to the example of human and potentialities
Consider 'human' defined as 'rational animal'. For knowledge: 'rational' implies potential for thought, so the implication is immediate (specific sense). For writing: you need to additionally conceptualize writing as reflective movement, so it requires a middle term (general sense).
Al-Sharnubi adds that the sharih meant the combination of both potentialities is bayyin in the general sense, not each separately.
4
Practice with another example from fiqh
Think of 'khamr' (wine) and 'intoxication'. The term 'khamr' by definition implies intoxication (specific sense). For 'nabidh' (date wine), the implication requires additional premises (e.g., it intoxicates like khamr) so it is in the general sense.
This distinction helps in analogical reasoning (qiyas).

Source Text

واللُّزوم بهذا المعنى بين المعنى الموضوع له وبين القابلية المذكورة ظاهرُ لا مِريَةَ فيه، فإنَّ العقل بعد تصوُّرِ الإنسان والقابلية المذكورة لا يتوقف في اللُّزوم بينهما. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : بهَذَا الْمَعْنَى) أي : المتلبس بهذا المعنى، وهو البين بالمعنى الأعم. (قَوْلُهُ: الْمَعْنَى الْمَوْضُوع لَهُ ) أي : الَّذي وضع له لفظ إنسان، وهو الحيوان الناطق . (قَوْلُهُ : الْقَابِلِيَّةِ الْمَذْكُورَةِ أي : قابليَّةِ الإنسان للكتابة والعِلْم، وقوله : (لا مرية فيه ؛ أي لا خفاءَ فيه، تأكيد لقوله : (ظاهر). (قَوْلُهُ : ظَاهِرٌ) خبرٌ عن قوله : (واللزوم)، وحيث كان ظاهراً لا خفاء فيه ؛ فيكونُ قولُهُ: (فإنَّ العقل . . . إلخ)؛ تنبيهاً لا دليلاً . فإنْ قُلْتَ : إِنَّهُ لا ينبّه إلا على ما كانَ فيهِ نوع خفاء، وقد نفاه بقوله : ظاهر، والجواب : أنَّ المراد بقوله : (ظاهر)؛ يعني : ظهوراً غير تامّ؛ فَلِذَا نَبَّه عليه . (قَوْلُهُ : لَا يَتَوَقَّفُ فِي اللُّزُوْمِ بَيْنَهُمَا ) أي : بل يجزم العقل باللُّزوم بينهما، هذا وما اقتضاه كلامه (۱) من أنَّ اللُّزومَ الكائنَ بينَ كلّ من قَبُولِ العِلْم والكتابةِ وبينَ الإنسانِ؛ ليس بيّناً بالمعنى الأخص ، بل بالمعنى الأعم ، فهو مُسلَّم بالنِّسبةِ لقَبُولِ الكتابة؛ لا بالنسبةِ لقَبُولِ العِلْمِ؛ إذْ هو لازم بين بالمعنى الأخص . وذلك لأنَّ الإنسانَ مَعناه حيوان ناطق، والناطق معناه المتفكِّرُ بالقوَّة، فإذا لاحظت الإنسانَ بهذا المعنى ؛ جزمَ العقل بلزوم قبولِهِ لِلعِلم لزوماً بيّناً بالمعنى الأخصّ، وإذا لاحظت الإنسانَ بهذا المعنى ؛ فلا يجزمُ العقلُ بلزوم قبولِ الكتابةِ له ، بلْ لَا بُدَّ مِن تصورِكَ زيادة على ذلك الكتابة بأنَّها الحركة المخصوصة المبنية على التَّأمل والرَّوِيَّة . العطار وقد علم أنّ البيِّنَ هو ما لا يفتقر إلى وسط وغير البيِّنِ مَا افتقر إليهِ؛ سواءٌ كَانَ الوسط واحداً أو مُتعدّداً، كما في الكنايات الكثيرة الوسائط. (۱) (قَوْلُهُ : وما اقتضاه كلامه ... إلخ) فيه أن الشارح لم يجعل كل واحد منهما لازماً بالمعنى الأعم حتى يردّ عليه بهذا بل مجموع الاثنين هو اللازم البين بالمعنى الأعم بدليل قوله : بل لا بُدَّ من تصورهما ....هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
And the implication in this sense between the meaning posited for [the word] and the mentioned potentiality is apparent, no doubt about it. For the intellect, after conceptualizing 'human' and the mentioned potentiality, does not hesitate in the implication between them. al-Dusuqi: His statement 'in this sense' means: that which is clothed in this sense, and it is the self-evident in the general sense. His statement 'the meaning posited for it' means: that for which the word 'human' is posited, and it is the rational animal. His statement 'the mentioned potentiality' means: the potentiality of the human for writing and knowledge. His statement 'no doubt about it' means: no concealment in it, an emphasis on his statement 'apparent'. His statement 'apparent' is a predicate of his statement 'the implication', and since it is apparent with no concealment, his statement 'for the intellect... etc.' is a notification, not a proof. If you say: one only notifies about something that has a type of concealment, and he denied that with his statement 'apparent', the answer: that what is meant by his statement 'apparent' is: an incomplete appearance; therefore he notified about it. His statement 'does not hesitate in the implication between them' means: rather the intellect judges with certainty the implication between them. This and what his statement (1) necessitated: that the implication existing between each of the potentiality of knowledge and writing and between 'human' is not self-evident in the specific sense, but in the general sense. It is conceded regarding the potentiality of writing, but not regarding the potentiality of knowledge; for it is a necessary implication self-evident in the specific sense. That is because 'human' means rational animal, and 'rational' means the one who thinks potentially. So when you consider 'human' in this sense, the intellect judges with certainty the implication of its receiving knowledge as a self-evident implication in the specific sense. And when you consider 'human' in this sense, the intellect does not judge with certainty the implication of its receiving writing, rather you must additionally conceptualize writing as the specific movement based on reflection and deliberation. al-'Attar: And it is known that the self-evident is that which does not need a middle term, and the non-self-evident is that which needs it, whether the middle term is one or multiple, as in the many mediated metonymies. (1) (His statement: 'And what his statement necessitated... etc.') In it is that the commentator did not make each one of them a necessary concomitant in the general sense so that this could be objected, rather the combination of the two is the self-evident necessary concomitant in the general sense, as evidenced by his statement: 'rather both must be conceptualized...' etc. al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the commentators on the sharih's statement about the implication (luzum) between the concept of 'human' and the potentiality for writing and knowledge. It begins with the sharih's claim that the implication in this sense is apparent (zahir) without doubt. Then al-Dusuqi explains the terms: 'in this sense' refers to the implication being bayyin (self-evident) in the general sense (al-ma'na al-a'mm). He clarifies that 'the meaning posited for it' is the rational animal (al-hayawan al-natiq), and 'the mentioned potentiality' is the potentiality for writing and knowledge. He then addresses a potential objection: if the implication is truly apparent with no concealment, why does the sharih need to notify about it? The answer is that 'zahir' here means an incomplete appearance, so notification is still useful. Next, al-Dusuqi explains that the sharih's later statement (referenced in footnote 1) that the implication between each of the potentialities and human is not bayyin in the specific sense (al-akhass) but in the general sense is accepted for writing but not for knowledge. Because the meaning of 'human' is rational animal, and 'rational' implies the potential for thought, which directly gives knowledge. So the intellect judges with certainty that human has the potential for knowledge as a self-evident implication in the specific sense. For writing, however, the intellect does not judge with certainty; one must additionally understand writing as a specific movement based on reflection. Then al-'Attar provides a definition: bayyin is that which does not require a middle term, while non-bayyin requires one, whether single or multiple, like metonymies with many intermediaries. Finally, al-Sharnubi notes that the sharih did not mean each potentiality separately, but their combination is the self-evident concomitant in the general sense. This passage guards against conflating different levels of logical implication. A common misunderstanding is to assume that all implications are equally self-evident. The example from fiqh: the term 'khamr' implies intoxication by definition, but 'nabidh' requires additional reasoning to establish implication.

Key Terms
اللزوم
البين بالمعنى الأعم
البين بالمعنى الأخص
القابلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) It is always immediate
D) It is disputed among scholars

Key Takeaway
The distinction between immediate (specific) and mediated (general) self-evidence is essential for precise logical analysis, as demonstrated by the refined analysis of human's potentialities for knowledge and writing.

The Dispute over the Condition of Implication in Dalalah Iltizamiyyah
The author opens a fasl on the three indications and discusses a disputed condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of the muhaqqiqun and others regarding whether the implication in dalalah iltizamiyyah must be self-evident in the general or specific sense, and explain the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
The verifiers require specific self-evidence for implicative indication.

This passage opens a new section on the three types of indication. The author praises a previous answer but notes a condition that is disputed: whether implication must be self-evident in the general sense (bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'mm) or only in the specific sense (al-akhass). The muhaqqiqun (verifiers) take a strict position, rejecting the general sense. This lesson unpacks the debate and its implications for logical definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key dispute
The passage states that a previous answer requires considering implication self-evident in the general sense, but this is disputed. Some say the specific sense is needed. Locate the Arabic phrases that indicate this contrast.
Look for 'لكنه مختلف فيه' and 'بل المحققون على أن هذا اللزوم غير معتبر'.
2
Understand the muhaqqiqun's position
The verifiers (muhaqqiqun) hold that the general sense is not sufficient; only the specific self-evident implication is valid. This is a stricter requirement that affects what counts as a valid implicative indication.
3
Analyze the distinction between conditionality and sufficiency
Al-Muhanni discusses two interpretations: if 'consideration' means conditionality (ishtirat), then requiring the specific necessarily includes the general (as per al-Fanari). But if it means sufficiency (kifaya), then the general might not suffice. This is a separate debate between al-Imam and the majority.
Grasp the difference: condition vs sufficiency in logical requirements.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Consider the term 'house' (bayt) implying 'protection'. Under the specific sense, the implication must be self-evident (intellectual or customary). Under the general sense, any implication would do. Which would the muhaqqiqun accept?
Example from the common misunderstanding: not all implications are created equal.

(قَوْلُهُ : بَل الْمُحَقِّقُوْنَ) إضراب انتقالي . العطّار (قَوْلُهُ : هَذَا الْجَوَابَ حَسَنٌ أَي : من جهةِ كَونِهِ أبطل المعارضة بمنع مقدمة دليلها . Section: On the definition of the three indications and their rulings. And know that this answer is good, except that it requires considering the implication self-evident in the general sense in the implicative indication, but it is disputed. Rather, the verifiers (1) are of the view that this implication is not considered valid, and the considered valid is al-luzum. al-Dusuqi: His statement 'good' means: because it repels the mentioned investigation. His statement 'except that it requires considering the implication... etc.' means: such that it would be sufficient in it. And his statement 'but it' means: considering the implication self-evident in the general sense in the implicative indication is disputed; some say it is, and some say it is not, and that what is considered valid in it is only the self-evident in the specific sense. And the commentator's repelling by the mentioned emendation [creates] the illusion that what the mentioned answer necessitated, that considering the implication self-evident in the general sense in the implicative indication is an agreed-upon matter. His statement 'in the general sense' means: that which is clothed in the general sense of the universal's relation to its parts. His statement 'rather the verifiers' is a transitional digression. al-'Attar: His statement 'this answer is good' means: from the perspective that it invalidated the objection by preventing the premise of its proof. His statement 'except that it requires considering the implication... etc.' al-Muhanni discussed it, the gist of which: if he intends its consideration in conditionality, then there is no harm in it, because al-Fanari stated in his commentary on Isaghuji that conditioning the specific necessitates conditioning the general, due to the impossibility of the specific without the general; thus the general meaning is also a condition. And the exemplification is for it, not for the specific. And to this extent the exemplification is valid. As for the sufficiency of the general meaning for the implication to be acceptable and its insufficiency, that is another discussion between al-Imam and the majority. And if he intends its consideration in sufficiency, then there is nothing in the exemplification that necessitates it. (1) (The commentator's statement: 'Rather the verifiers... etc.') It comes in al-'Attar from al-Harawi at the statement of the author: 'And it is necessary that the implication be intellectual or customary, that it is not permissible to drop the customary implication from consideration, otherwise it would necessitate excluding the metaphors and metonymies considered valid in discourses, leading to a constriction in the matter of implicative indication which the general rules of the art reject.' End quote. For if it is said: custom differs according to habit, it is replied that conventional indication differs according to conventions, and no one has said to drop it from consideration due to this difference.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage opens a new section (fasl) on the definition of the three types of indication (dalalah) and their rulings. The author directly addresses a previous answer, calling it good, but points out that it requires considering the implication (luzum) in the implicative indication (dalalah iltizamiyyah) to be self-evident in the general sense (bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'mm). However, this is a point of dispute. The muhaqqiqun (verifiers) hold that such implication is not considered valid; rather, what is valid is the implication self-evident in the specific sense (al-akhass). Al-Dusuqi explains that the answer repels the investigation, and that the commentator's emendation might wrongly suggest agreement. He clarifies that the condition is disputed: some accept the general sense, others only the specific. The phrase 'in the general sense' refers to the universal's relation to its parts. Then al-'Attar adds a detailed discussion: he quotes al-Muhanni who explains that if the requirement is for conditionality, requiring the specific always includes the general, as per al-Fanari's commentary on Isaghuji, so exemplification is valid. But if the requirement is for sufficiency (whether the general sense suffices for the implication to be acceptable), that is a separate debate between al-Imam and the majority. The footnote from al-Harawi emphasizes that even customary implication must be considered, otherwise many valid metaphors and metonymies would be excluded, which the general principles of logic (al-fann) must accommodate. Thus the passage guards against overly restrictive views of dalalah iltizamiyyah. A common misunderstanding is to think that only intellectual implication is valid, but custom also plays a role. An example from Islamic law: the term 'bayt' (house) may imply 'protection' by custom, even if not strictly intellectual.

The Role of Customary Implication in Logical Indication
A footnote defending customary implication against exclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to argue for the inclusion of customary implication (al-luzum al-'urfi) in dalalah iltizamiyyah and explain how it preserves valid metaphors and metonymies in Islamic discourse.

Core Idea
Customary implication is indispensable; excluding it would cripple logical analysis of figurative language.

This passage concludes the section by addressing an important objection: can customary implication (al-luzum al-'urfi) be dropped from consideration? Al-Harawi argues forcefully that it must be retained, otherwise many valid metaphors and metonymies (majaz and kinayah) would be excluded from logical analysis. He compares it to conventional indication (dalalah wad'iyyah), which varies by convention yet is never discarded. This lesson explores the necessity of customary implication in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim of al-Harawi
Al-Harawi states that it is not permissible to drop customary implication from consideration. Find the Arabic phrase that expresses this prohibition.
Look for 'لا يسوغ إسقاط اللزوم العرفي من الاعتبار'.
2
Understand the consequence of dropping customary implication
If only intellectual implication were accepted, then all metaphors and metonymies that rely on customary association would be invalid in logical arguments. This would severely restrict the scope of dalalah iltizamiyyah.
3
Analyze the rebuttal to the objection about varying custom
An objector might say that custom differs, making implication unreliable. Al-Harawi replies that conventional indication (dalalah wad'iyyah) also varies by convention (e.g., different languages), yet no one discards it. By analogy, customary implication should also be retained.
The argument uses qiyas: comparing customary implication to conventional indication.
4
Apply the principle to an Islamic example
Consider the metaphor 'the sea' for a generous man. The implication of generosity is customary, not intellectual. According to al-Harawi, this should be accepted as valid dalalah iltizamiyyah.
Example from Arabic rhetoric.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في تعريف الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها وَاعلَمْ أنَّ هذا الجوابَ حَسَنُ، إِلَّا أَنَّه يوجِبُ اعتبارَ اللُّزومِ البيِّنِ بالمعنى الأعم في الدَّلالةِ الالتزاميَّة، لكنَّه مختلف فيه. بل المحققونَ (١) على أنَّ هذا اللُّزومُ غيرُ معتبر، والمعتبر هو الدسوقي اللُّزوم (قَوْلُهُ : حَسَنٌ أي : لأنَّه يدفعُ البحث المذكور . ) (قَوْلُهُ : إِلَّا أَنَّهُ يُوجِبُ اعْتِبَارَ اللُّزُوم ... إلخ) أي: بحيث يكون كافياً فيها ، وقوله : (لكنَّه)؛ أي: اعتبار اللُّزوم البيّن بالمعنى الأعم في الدَّلالةِ الالتزاميَّةِ مُختلفٌ فيه ؛ فَبعضُهُم قالَ ،به وبعضُهُم قالَ ،بعدمِهِ وأنَّ المعتبر فيها إنَّما هو البيِّنُ بالمعنى الأخصّ، ودفعُ الشَّارحِ بالاستدراك المذكورِ توهُمُ أنَّ مَا اقتضاءَ الجَوابُ المذكورُ مِن أنَّ اعتبارَ اللَّزوم البيّن بالمعنى الأعمّ في الدَّلالة الالتزاميَّةِ ؛ أمرٌ مُتَّفَقٌ عليه . (قَوْلُهُ : بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَعَم أي : المتلبس بالمعنى الأعم من التباس الكلِّي بجزئيه . (قَوْلُهُ : بَل الْمُحَقِّقُوْنَ) إضراب انتقالي . العطّار (قَوْلُهُ : هَذَا الْجَوَابَ حَسَنٌ أَي : من جهةِ كَونِهِ أبطل المعارضة بمنع مقدمة دليلها . (قَوْلُهُ : إِلَّا أَنَّهُ يُوجِبُ اعْتِبَارَ الْلُّزُوْمِ ... إِلَخ) بحث فيه المحنِّي بِمَا حَاصِلُهُ إِنْ أرادَ اعتبارَهُ في الاشتراط؛ فَلَا ضرر فيهِ لِمَا صرَّحَ بِهِ الفناريُّ في شَرْحِ «إيساغوجي» بأنَّ اشتراط الأخص يُوجِبُ اشتراط الأعم؛ لِعَدَم تَحقُّقِ الأخص بدونِ الأعم، فيكون المعنى الأعم أيضاً شَرطاً ، والتَّمثيلُ لهُ ؛ لا للأخص، وبِهَذا القدر يصحُ التمثيلُ، فأمَّا كفاية المعنى الأعمّ لِكَوْنِ الالتزام مقبولاً وعدم كفايتِهِ؛ فَبَحْثُ آخَرُ فِيهِ بين الإمام والجمهور، وإن أراد اعتباره في الكفاية؛ فَلَيسَ في التَّمثيل ما يقتضيه . (۱) (قولُ الشَّارحِ : بل المحققون... إلخ) يأتي في العطار عن الهروي عند قول المصنف. ولا بد من اللزوم عقلاً أو عرفاً أنَّه لا يسوغ إسقاط اللزوم العرفي من الاعتبار وإلا لزم خروج المجازات والكنايات المعتبرة في المحاورات مع إفضائه إلى ضيق في أمر الدلالة الالتزامية يأباه عموم قواعد الفنّ ا.هـ.. فإن قيل : إن العرف يختلف بحسب العادة رد بأن الدلالة . الوضعية تختلف باختلاف الأوضاع ولم يقل أحد بسقوطها عن الاعتبار بهذا الاختلاف =
Section: On the definition of the three indications and their rulings. And know that this answer is good, except that it requires considering the implication self-evident in the general sense in the implicative indication, but it is disputed. Rather, the verifiers (1) are of the view that this implication is not considered valid, and the considered valid is al-luzum. al-Dusuqi: His statement 'good' means: because it repels the mentioned investigation. His statement 'except that it requires considering the implication... etc.' means: such that it would be sufficient in it. And his statement 'but it' means: considering the implication self-evident in the general sense in the implicative indication is disputed; some say it is, and some say it is not, and that what is considered valid in it is only the self-evident in the specific sense. And the commentator's repelling by the mentioned emendation [creates] the illusion that what the mentioned answer necessitated, that considering the implication self-evident in the general sense in the implicative indication is an agreed-upon matter. His statement 'in the general sense' means: that which is clothed in the general sense of the universal's relation to its parts. His statement 'rather the verifiers' is a transitional digression. al-'Attar: His statement 'this answer is good' means: from the perspective that it invalidated the objection by preventing the premise of its proof. His statement 'except that it requires considering the implication... etc.' al-Muhanni discussed it, the gist of which: if he intends its consideration in conditionality, then there is no harm in it, because al-Fanari stated in his commentary on Isaghuji that conditioning the specific necessitates conditioning the general, due to the impossibility of the specific without the general; thus the general meaning is also a condition. And the exemplification is for it, not for the specific. And to this extent the exemplification is valid. As for the sufficiency of the general meaning for the implication to be acceptable and its insufficiency, that is another discussion between al-Imam and the majority. And if he intends its consideration in sufficiency, then there is nothing in the exemplification that necessitates it. (1) (The commentator's statement: 'Rather the verifiers... etc.') It comes in al-'Attar from al-Harawi at the statement of the author: 'And it is necessary that the implication be intellectual or customary, that it is not permissible to drop the customary implication from consideration, otherwise it would necessitate excluding the metaphors and metonymies considered valid in discourses, leading to a constriction in the matter of implicative indication which the general rules of the art reject.' End quote. For if it is said: custom differs according to habit, it is replied that conventional indication differs according to conventions, and no one has said to drop it from consideration due to this difference.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage opens a new section (fasl) on the definition of the three types of indication (dalalah) and their rulings. The author directly addresses a previous answer, calling it good, but points out that it requires considering the implication (luzum) in the implicative indication (dalalah iltizamiyyah) to be self-evident in the general sense (bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'mm). However, this is a point of dispute. The muhaqqiqun (verifiers) hold that such implication is not considered valid; rather, what is valid is the implication self-evident in the specific sense (al-akhass). Al-Dusuqi explains that the answer repels the investigation, and that the commentator's emendation might wrongly suggest agreement. He clarifies that the condition is disputed: some accept the general sense, others only the specific. The phrase 'in the general sense' refers to the universal's relation to its parts. Then al-'Attar adds a detailed discussion: he quotes al-Muhanni who explains that if the requirement is for conditionality, requiring the specific always includes the general, as per al-Fanari's commentary on Isaghuji, so exemplification is valid. But if the requirement is for sufficiency (whether the general sense suffices for the implication to be acceptable), that is a separate debate between al-Imam and the majority. The footnote from al-Harawi emphasizes that even customary implication must be considered, otherwise many valid metaphors and metonymies would be excluded, which the general principles of logic (al-fann) must accommodate. Thus the passage guards against overly restrictive views of dalalah iltizamiyyah. A common misunderstanding is to think that only intellectual implication is valid, but custom also plays a role. An example from Islamic law: the term 'bayt' (house) may imply 'protection' by custom, even if not strictly intellectual.

Key Terms
اللزوم العرفي
المجازات
الكنايات
الدلالة الوضعية

Checkpoint

A) All implications would become invalid

C) Intellectual implication would become dominant
D) The rules of logic would become too broad

Key Takeaway
Customary implication is a necessary component of dalalah iltizamiyyah, as excluding it would invalidate many accepted figurative uses of language that are central to Islamic discourse.

The Two Senses of Evident Entailment in Implicative Signification
Refining the entailment condition for implicative signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the general and specific senses of evident entailment (al-luzum al-bayyin) and identify which one the muhaqqiqun require for implicative signification.

Core Idea
Not all evident entailments are equal — the muhaqqiqun demand the specific sense for valid implicative signification.

In this passage, the commentator refines a key condition for implicative signification (dalala iltizamiyya). He acknowledges a previous answer is 'good' but points out a critical flaw: it requires evident entailment in the general sense. The verified scholars (al-muhaqqiqun) reject this and insist on the specific sense. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly assessing whether a word’s connotation is truly necessary or merely conventional.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial position
The commentator says a certain answer would require evident entailment in the general sense. What is that answer referring to? It is about the condition for implicative signification.
2
Find the muhaqqiqun's objection
They reject the general sense and require the specific sense. The specific sense is where merely conceiving the malzum (subject-matter) suffices for the mind to grasp the necessary relation.
3
Examine the example
The entailment of sight for blindness: sight is part of the definition of blindness, so conceiving blindness automatically brings sight to mind. This illustrates the specific sense.
4
Contrast the two definitions
According to one interpretation, the general and specific senses are disparate; according to the commentator's own, they have generality/specificity in individuals, not in concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعلَمْ أنَّ هذا الجوابَ حَسَنُ، إِلَّا أَنَّه يوجِبُ اعتبارَ اللُّزومِ البيِّنِ بالمعنى الأعم في الدَّلالةِ الالتزاميَّة، لكنَّه مختلف فيه. بل المحققون على أنَّ هذا اللُّزومَ غيرُ معتبر، والمعتبر هو البين بالمعنى الأخص، وهو الذي يكفي فيه تصور الملزوم فقط جزم العقل باللزوم. قَوْلُهُ: بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَخَصَّ أي : كلزوم البصرِ لِلعمى، فإِنَّهُ لازم له؛ لأنَّه متى تَصوَّر العمى الَّذي هو الملزوم؛ تصوّر اللازم الذي هو البصر؛ لأنَّه مأخوذ في تعريفه، وَلَا بُدَّ مِن معرفةِ كُلِّ جزء من أجزاء التَّعريف حتَّى يعلم المعرَّف. قَوْلُهُ : يَكْفِي فِيهِ أي : في جزمِ العقل باللزوم. واعلَمْ أَنَّهُ على ما ذكرَهُ في تعريف الأخص والأعم؛ لا يتأَنَّى خصوصٌ وَلَا عموم، بل عليه يكونان مُتباينين، وأمَّا مَا ذكرنَاهُ؛ فَالخصوص والعموم عليه ظاهر كمَا عُلِمَ، فالأعمِّيَّةُ في الأفراد لا في المفهوم.
And know that this answer is good, except that it requires considering evident entailment in the general sense in implicative signification, but it is disputed. Rather, the verified scholars hold that this entailment is not considered, and the considered one is the evident in the specific sense, which is that in which merely conceiving the subject-matter suffices for the mind's certainty of the entailment. His statement: 'in the specific sense' means: like the entailment of sight for blindness, for it is necessary for it because whenever one conceives blindness, which is the subject-matter, one conceives the necessary, which is sight, because it is taken in its definition, and one must know every part of the definition in order to know the defined. His statement: 'suffices in it' means: in the mind's certainty of the entailment. And know that according to what he mentioned in the definition of the specific and the general, there is no particularity or generality, rather on that basis they are disparate. But according to what we mentioned, particularity and generality are apparent as known. So the generality is in individuals, not in concept.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement on the definition of implicative signification (dalala iltizamiyya). The commentator begins by acknowledging that a certain answer is 'good' but points out a flaw: it requires that the entailment (luzum) be evident in the general sense (al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'am). This requirement is disputed. The verified scholars (al-muhaqqiqun) hold that the required entailment is not this general one, but rather the evident in the specific sense (al-akhass), where merely conceiving the subject-matter (malzum) is sufficient for the mind to necessarily conceive the entailment (lazim). The commentator exemplifies this with the entailment of sight for blindness: whenever one conceives blindness, one necessarily conceives sight because sight is part of the definition of blindness. The commentator then clarifies that according to the initial definition, the general and specific senses are disparate (mutabayin), but according to the alternative explanation, there is a relationship of generality and specificity, where the general sense encompasses more individuals (afrad) even if the concepts differ. The argument guards against conflating the condition of entailment: the specific sense is actually a stricter condition, but it is the one accepted by the verified scholars for the validity of implicative signification.

Key Terms
الدلالة الالتزامية
اللزوم البيّن
المحققون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the muhaqqiqun in this passage, what is required for implicative signification?
A) Evident entailment in the general sense (al-a'am).
B) Evident entailment in the specific sense (al-akhass).
C) Any type of entailment, whether evident or not.
D) No entailment at all; mere convention suffices.

Key Takeaway
For implicative signification to be valid, the entailment must be such that merely conceiving the subject-matter forces the mind to conceive the necessary concomitant — this is the specific sense of evident entailment.

Correcting the Example for Necessary Indication
Author corrects the example for necessary indication from pair to blindness-sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why an example of necessary implication may be inadequate and select a better example that satisfies the condition of immediate mental apprehension.

Core Idea
Necessary indication demands an example where the implied idea cannot be forgotten.

In Islamic logic, examples are not arbitrary; they must clarify the logical point. The commentators here correct the standard example for necessary indication (dalala iltizamiyya) from 'two' indicating 'twoness' to 'blindness' indicating 'sight'. This correction sharpens our understanding of what 'necessary' means in this context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original example
The passage states that the common example is 'pairing of two' (zawjiyyat al-ithnayn) as an instance of necessary indication. Note that this is presented as what was previously accepted.
The example appears in standard logic texts before this correction.
2
Recognise the critique
The commentator argues that conceiving of 'two' does not necessarily bring 'twoness' to mind; one might be heedless of the pairing concept. This shows that the implication is not immediate or certain.
The mind may be occupied elsewhere, so the connection is not 'clear' (bayyin).
3
Understand the correction
A better example is 'blindness' (al-ama) indicating 'sight' (al-basar), because as soon as one conceives of blindness, the concept of sight is immediately present. No additional reflection is needed.
Blindness is defined by absence of sight, making the dependence obvious.
4
Extract the condition
The key condition for necessary indication is that the implication (luzum) must be 'clear' (bayyin) in the sense that the implied meaning (lazim) is inseparable from the antecedent (malzum) in the mind. The example must trigger the implied meaning instantly.
This is called 'clear implication in the most specific sense' (al-luzum al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قَوْلُهُ : لَكِنْ غَرَضُنَا ... إلخ) هو واقع موقع خبر المبتدأ الذي هو قوله : (وهذا (البحث. وأصلُ الكلام وهذا البحثُ وَإِنْ كانَ مناقشة في المثال؛ لا بأس به، وإِنَّما يكونُ بِهِ بأس إذا لم يكن غرضُنَا به شيئاً ، لكن غرضُنَا بِهِ التَّنبيه . . . إلخ، ونظير هذا قولك : زيدٌ وَإِنْ كانَ غنيًّا؛ لكنَّه بخيل ؛ أي: زيدٌ وإِنْ كانَ غنيًّا؛ لا يُعطي شيئاً، فيحسب له، وإنَّما يُعطي شيئاً إذا لم يكن بخيلاً، لكنَّه بخيل. العطار (قَوْلُهُ : فَالصَّوابُ أَنْ يُمَثَّلَ مُرتبط بقولِهِ : بل المحققون، ثمَّ إِنْ أخذ الانقسام بمُتسا ويَيْنِ وسطاً غير ضار في كونِ اللُّزوم بيناً؛ لأَنَّهُ لَا يَغِيبُ عَنِ الذَّهَنِ مَتى تَصُورَ الملزوم؛ فهو كالقضايا التي قياساتها معها.
