Chapter: On the definition of the three indications and their rulings. The correct view is that it should be exemplified by the pairing of two. And this discussion, although it is a critique of the example, and it is not the habit of students, for in exemplification the hypothetical suffices, whether it conforms to reality or not; but our purpose from al-Dasooqi (his statement: 'the correct view... etc.') is a branch of his statement: 'rather the verifiers.' (His statement: 'by the pairing of two') means: because when we conceive of two, we conceive of pairing, but it may be said: conceiving of two does not necessitate conceiving of pairing, let alone the mind's certainty of its implication, because the mind may be heedless of conceiving pairing. Thus it was transmitted from 'Aj. And then the better exemplification is the indication of blindness to sight, because from the conception of blindness, the conception of sight necessarily follows. (His statement: 'and his bringing it') is a substitution of the branch by conjunction. (His statement: 'and this discussion although... etc.') is an answer to what is said: this discussion is a discussion of the example, and it is not the habit of the accomplished. His statement: 'although it was...' the waw is for state. (His statement: 'by the habit') means: by the custom of students, plural of student. (His statement: 'for the hypothetical suffices in exemplification... etc.') is a cause for his statement: 'and it is not... etc.' (His statement: 'the hypothetical') means: the estimation, i.e., estimation of correctness. (His statement: 'but our purpose... etc.') is in the position of the predicate of the subject which is his statement: 'and this (discussion). The original wording: 'And this discussion, although it is a critique of the example, is not objectionable, and it is only objectionable if we had no purpose in it, but our purpose in it is the notification... etc.' An analogy: your statement: 'Zayd, although he is wealthy, is stingy'; i.e., Zayd, although he is wealthy, gives nothing, and thus he is accounted stingy, and he only gives something if he is not stingy, but he is stingy. al-Attar (His statement: 'the correct view is that it should be exemplified' is connected to his statement: 'rather the verifiers', then if the division is taken with equal middles, it does not harm that the implication is clear, because it does not disappear from the mind whenever the antecedent is conceived; it is like propositions whose syllogisms are with them. (His statement: 'his bringing') means: the discussion. (His statement: 'the notification that what is considered') means: on the answer that... etc., and its answer (what is considered) is the clear implication in the more specific sense. (His statement: 'any implication') is a deferred subject, and his statement: 'what is considered' is a forwarded predicate, and this sentence is 'that', and its pronoun is elided.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of the three types of verbal indication, specifically correcting the example used for necessary indication (al-dalala al-iltizamiyya). The original example of 'two' indicating 'twoness' (pairing) is criticized because simply conceiving of 'two' does not necessarily bring 'twoness' to mind; one might be heedless of the concept of pairing. Therefore, a better example is 'blindness' indicating 'sight', because as soon as one conceives of blindness, the idea of sight is immediately present. The passage also justifies why such a discussion on examples is not beneath scholars: even though hypothetical examples suffice, the purpose here is to clarify what is meant by 'clear implication' (al-luzum al-bayyin). The commentator al-Dasooqi explains that the discussion is a branch of the verifiers' view, and al-Attar adds that the example is acceptable if the equal middles are taken, and the implication is as clear as propositions that carry their own proofs. Finally, the passage emphasizes that the considered factor is the clear implication in the most specific sense.

اللُّزوم البين
الملزوم
اللازم

Key Takeaway

Classifying General Term to Individual under Tadammun
Author defends the threefold classification against an objection regarding general terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any indication of a general term to a specific individual under one of the three types of conventional verbal indication, recognizing when it falls under tadammun (implication).

Core Idea
A general term indicating an individual is implication (tadammun), not an exception to the three types.

The threefold classification of conventional verbal indication—correspondence (mutabaqa), implication (tadammun), and necessary (iltizam)—seems exhaustive. But an objection arises: how does a general term like 'my slaves' indicate a specific individual like Zayd? This lesson examines the objection and its correct resolution, showing that such indication is in fact a valid form of implication indication, not a gap in the classification.

Guided Path
1
Review the three types of dalaala
Recall the definitions: mutabaqa (word indicates full meaning), tadammun (word indicates a part of its meaning), iltizam (word indicates an external meaning necessarily connected). Understand that tadammun traditionally involves a part-whole relationship, e.g., 'house' indicating 'room'.
2
Identify the objection
Consider the general term 'my slaves' indicating a particular slave Zayd. Does this fit any type? Not mutabaqa (Zayd is not the whole meaning), not tadammun (Zayd is a particular, not a part in the usual sense), not iltizam (Zayd is not external to the meaning). So the objection claims an omission in the classification.
The objection is based on a narrow interpretation of 'part' as only a conceptual component, not an individual member.
3
Examine the proposed answers
Some responded that it is mutabaqa because 'my slaves came' is equivalent to a set of propositions like 'Zayd came, Amr came...'. But the author rejects this: equivalence in meaning does not entail the same type of indication.
The truth lies elsewhere, as the author states.
4
Understand the correct classification: tadammun
The author argues it is tadammun because Zayd is part of the collective entity (al-hay'a al-mujtama'a) composed of the individuals indicated by the word. Though Zayd is a particular in his essence, he is a part of the collective. Thus the word implies him as a part.
This extends the notion of 'part' to include individuals of a collective.
5
Apply the rule to other examples
Test the reasoning: If 'the students' indicates 'Ahmad', that is tadammun because Ahmad is a member of the student body. This classification preserves the exhaustiveness of the three types.
Ensure you can defend this classification against the objection.

تنبيه : أورد على حصرِ الدَّلالة اللفظيَّةِ الوضعيَّةِ في الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ الَّتِي ذكرَهَا المصنِّفُ ; دلالةَ العامّ على بعض أفرادِهِ، كَدلالة عبيدي من قولك : جاءَ عبيدي، على زيد، فإنَّها ليست مطابقيَّةً ; لأنَّ زيداً ليس تمام المعنى الموضوع له اللفظ ، وَلَا ضمنيَّة ; لأنَّ زيداً جزئي لا جزء، وَلَا التزاميَّة ; لأنَّهُ ليس خارجاً عن الموضوع له . وأجابَ بعضُهُم بأنَّها مُطابقيَّةٌ; لأنَّ : جاءَ عبيدي في قوَّةِ قضايا بعدد أفراده؛ أي : جاءَ زيد وجاءَ عمرو ... إلخ . والحقُّ : أنَّها تضمنيَّة ; لأنَّ زيداً ; وإن كان جزئيا باعتبار ذاتِهِ; إِلَّا أَنَّهُ جزءٌ مِنَ الهيئة المجتمعةِ مِنَ الأفرادِ الدَّالّ عليها اللَّفْظُ، وَلَا يلزمُ مِن كونِ الشَّيء في قوَّةِ الشَّيء أَنَّهُ يدل دلالته . (قَوْلُهُ : لَمَّا كَانَتْ دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى الْخَارج) أي : على المعنى ... الخارج عن الموضوع له; سواء كان ذلك الخارج وجوديًّا ; كالحياةِ اللازمة للعِلْمِ، أو عدميًّا ; كعدم الفرس اللازم للإنسان، أو اعتباريا ; كَالأبوَّةِ اللازمة للبنوة. العطار (قَوْلُهُ : دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى الْخَارج) أي : المعنى الخارج عن المعنى المطابقي; أي: الَّذي لم يعتبر وضع اللفظ له، وليس المراد بالخارج; مَا هُوَ خَارِجَ الذَّهَنِ كَمَا قَدْ يُتوهَّمُ، واعْلَمْ أَنَّ مَذهب الكثيرِ مِنَ المحققين ; مِنهُم الشَّيخُ الرَّئيسُ (۱) (۱) هو ابن سينا .
A notification: An objection was raised against restricting conventional verbal indication to the three types mentioned by the author: the indication of the general term to some of its individuals, like the indication of 'my slaves' from your statement 'my slaves came' to Zayd. For it is not correspondence indication because Zayd is not the entire meaning for which the word was coined; nor implication indication because Zayd is a particular, not a part; nor necessary indication because he is not external to the meaning. And some answered that it is correspondence indication because 'my slaves came' is in the strength of propositions equal to the number of its individuals: i.e., 'Zayd came' and 'Amr came' etc. But the truth is that it is implication indication, because Zayd, although he is a particular in his essence, is nevertheless a part of the collective entity composed of individuals indicated by the word. And it does not follow from the fact that something is in the strength of something that it is indicated by its indication. (His statement: 'since the indication of the word to the external') means: to the meaning... external to the subject matter; whether that external is existential like life necessary for knowledge, or privative like non-existence of making necessary for man, or relational like fatherhood necessary for sonship. al-Attar (His statement: 'the indication of the word to the external') means: the meaning external to the correspondence meaning; i.e., that which was not intended by the coinage of the word. And what is meant by 'external' is not what is external to the mind as might be imagined. And know that the doctrine of many of the verifiers, among them al-Shaykh al-Ra'is (1) (1) He is Ibn Sina.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection to the classification of conventional verbal indication into three types: correspondence (mutabaqa), implication (tadammun), and necessary (iltizam). The objection concerns the indication of a general term like 'my slaves' to a specific individual like Zayd. This indication does not fit any of the three: it is not correspondence because Zayd is not the full meaning; not implication because Zayd is a particular, not a part; not necessary because Zayd is not external to the meaning. Some respond that it is correspondence because the statement is equivalent to a set of propositions. The correct response, however, is that it is implication (tadminiyya) because Zayd, though a particular, is part of the collective entity denoted by the term. The passage then clarifies that 'external' in necessary indication means external to the primary meaning, not external to the mind. Finally, it notes that many verifiers, including Ibn Sina, hold this view.

C) Necessary indication (iltizam) because Zayd is necessarily implied.
D) It is not covered by any of the three types.

Key Takeaway
A general term indicating an individual is not an exception to the three types; it falls under tadammun because the individual is a part of the collective entity denoted by the term.

The Three Types of Signification: Conformity, Containment, and Implication
Defines three significations and their conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conformity, containment, and implicative signification, and identify the condition required for each using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Words signify meaning directly, by parts, or by necessary implication—each with its own conditions.

In Islamic logic and usul al-fiqh, understanding how words convey meaning is essential for interpreting sacred texts correctly. This passage from the Tadhhib defines the three types of signification—conformity (mutabaqa), containment (tadamun), and implicative (iltizam)—and specifies the conditions under which each operates. The key insight is that only implicative signification requires a mental necessary connection (luzum dhihni) between the word's meaning and the implied external matter, as exemplified by 'two' implying 'evenness'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
From the passage, list the three types: conformity (dalala mutabaqa), containment (dalala tadamuniyya), and implicative (dalala iltizamiyya). Understand their definitions as given.
Conformity: the word signifies the exact meaning for which it was placed. Containment: the word signifies a part of its composite meaning. Implicative: the word signifies an external matter necessarily connected to the primary meaning.
2
Extract the condition (shart) for each type
Note that only implicative signification has a specific condition: a mental necessary connection (luzum dhihni). Conformity requires only knowledge of the linguistic convention (wad'), even for ambiguous words (mushtarak). Containment requires no extra condition beyond the word being placed for a composite meaning.
The passage states: 'فلا بدَّ للدلالة الالتزامية من شرط، اللزوم الذهني' and for the other two, 'لا تحتاج أيضاً إلى اشتراط'.
3
Understand the mental necessary connection (luzum dhihni)
Focus on the meaning of 'mental necessary connection': the external matter must be conceptually inseparable from the primary meaning such that understanding the primary meaning necessitates understanding the external matter. The example given is the connection between 'two' and 'evenness'—a rational necessity.
The commentator clarifies that external connection (luzum khariji) is not required; it is a mental/rational connection.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Take the word 'al-alam' (العالم, the world/universe). Conformity: the entire created universe. Containment: individual components like heavens, earth, etc. Implicative: the necessary existence of a Creator, because the world cannot exist without a maker—a rational necessity.
This example demonstrates how the three significations operate on the same word. The implicative meaning (Creator) requires the mental necessary connection.
5
Distinguish the role of speaker's intent
Note that for conformity signification, the word signifies all possible meanings of a polysemous term (mushtarak) regardless of the speaker's intent. The passage says 'عدم علمه بمراد المتكلم غير قادح'—the hearer's lack of knowledge of the speaker's intent does not negate the signification. This is important in usul al-fiqh when interpreting ambiguous legal texts.
The commentator adds that contextual clues (qarina) determine intent, but that is separate from the signification itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في تعريف الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها. فَلا بُدَّ لِلدَّلالةِ على الخارج من شرط. ثمَّ المناسب أن يقول: فلا بُدَّ لِلدَّلالةِ الالتزاميَّةِ مِن شرط، اللُّزومُ الذّهني؛ أي: كونُ الأمرِ الخارجي لازماً للمعنى الموضوع له اللَّفْظُ الذهنِ؛ بحيثُ يلزمُ مِن إدراك المسمَّى إدراكه. وإنّما اشترط هذا في دلالة الالتزام؛ لأنَّه لو لم يوجد؛ امتنع فَهُمُ الأمر الخارجي من اللفظ. ولا بدَّ في الدلالة الالتزامية من اللزوم بين مسمى اللفظ والخارج إما عقلًا كاللزوم بين الاثنين والزوجية، ولا يشترط اللزوم الخارجي. وأما الدلالة على المعنى الموضوع له أعني المطابقة فيكفي فيها العلم بالوضع ولو في المشترك، فإنه إذا سمع اللفظ المشترك ينتقل ذهنه لملاحظة معانيه بأسرها، ويكون اللفظ دالاً عليها مطابقة، وعدم علمه بمراد المتكلم غير قادح؛ لأن الدلالة هي الفهم، وهذه المعاني مفهومة من اللفظ عند إطلاقه. وأما الدلالة التضمنية فلا تحتاج أيضاً إلى اشتراط؛ لأن اللفظ إذا وضع لمعنى كلي كان دالاً على كل واحد من أجزائه دلالة تضمنية؛ لأن فهم الجزء لازم لفهم الكل المركب، ولا يمكن أن يكون اللفظ موضوعاً لخصوصية معنى مركب من أجزاء غير متناهية حتى يلزم دلالة اللفظ الواحد على أمور غير متناهية.
Chapter on the definition of the three significations and their rulings. There must be for signification of the external [matter] a condition. Then the appropriate thing is to say: There must be for implicative signification a condition, [namely] mental necessary connection; i.e., that the external matter be a necessary concomitant of the meaning for which the word is placed in the mind, such that from apprehending the named thing, its apprehension follows. He stipulated this condition only for implicative signification because if it did not exist, understanding the external matter from the word would be impossible. And it is necessary in implicative signification that there be a necessary connection between the word's named thing and the external [matter], either rational, like the necessary connection between two and evenness, and external necessary connection is not stipulated. As for signification of the meaning for which the word is placed, i.e., conformity, knowledge of the linguistic convention suffices for it, even in an ambiguous term; for when one hears the ambiguous term, his mind moves to notice all its meanings, and the word signifies them by conformity, and his not knowing the speaker's intent is not detrimental; because signification is understanding, and these meanings are understood from the word when it is uttered absolutely. As for containment signification, it also does not need a condition; because if the word is placed for a composite meaning, it signifies each of its parts by containment, because understanding the part is necessary for understanding the whole composite, and it is impossible for the word to be placed for the particularity of a composite meaning consisting of infinite parts, such that a single word would signify infinite things.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of linguistic signification—conformity (mutabaqa), containment (tadamun), and implicative (iltizam)—and specifies the conditions required for each. The author begins by stating that signification of an external matter (i.e., implicative) requires a condition. The commentator explains that this condition is mental necessary connection (luzum dhihni), meaning that the external matter is conceptually inseparable from the primary meaning. For example, the word 'two' implies 'evenness' in the mind. This condition is specific to implicative signification because without it, the external matter would not be understood from the word. The passage then clarifies that the necessary connection can be rational ('aqli) as in the example, and external necessary connection (luzum khariji) is not required. Next, the author discusses conformity signification, stating that knowledge of the linguistic convention (wad') is sufficient, even for ambiguous words (mushtarak). The commentator highlights that the speaker's intent is irrelevant to the signification itself; the word signifies all its possible meanings when uttered, and contextual clues (qarina) are needed to determine the intended meaning, but that is separate from the signification. Finally, containment signification requires no extra condition because a word placed for a composite meaning automatically signifies its parts (e.g., the word 'house' signifies both wall and roof). The commentator adds that the composite meaning cannot consist of infinite parts, otherwise a single word would signify an infinity of things, which is impossible.

C) An external, physical necessary connection between the named thing and the external matter
D) No condition is required because the word itself conveys the meaning

Key Takeaway
The three types of signification differ in their conditions: only implicative requires a mental necessary connection; the others rely on linguistic convention or composition.

Identify its conformity meaning (the ritual prayer), its containment meanings (the actions: ruku', sujud, etc.), and its implicative meaning (e.g., spiritual connection, which may or may not be a mental necessary connection—decide based on the condition discussed in the passage).

The Condition of Intellectual Concomitance in Implicative Signification
Commentators explain the requirement of intellectual concomitance for implicative signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between intellectual and external concomitance and determine whether a given implicative signification is valid.

Core Idea
Implicative signification requires intellectual necessity, not mere empirical correlation.

In this passage, the commentator explains the key condition for implicative signification (dalala iltizamiyya): the link between the term's primary meaning and the implied meaning must be intellectually necessary, not just empirically observed. This distinction is crucial for correct reasoning about implicit meanings in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Locate the phrase 'لا بد في الدلالة الالتزامية من اللزوم'. This tells you that implicative signification requires a necessary concomitance (luzum) between the term's meaning and the external meaning.
2
Distinguish the two types of concomitance
The passage mentions intellectual ('aqlan) and external (khariji) concomitance. Intellectual concomitance is based on conceptual necessity (e.g., two and evenness), while external is based on empirical coexistence (e.g., crows and blackness).
3
Analyze the two/evenness example
The example of 'two' and 'evenness' shows that the concept of two necessarily includes evenness. This is intellectual because the intellect cannot conceive of two without it being even.
4
Examine the blindness/sight example
Blindness is defined as the absence of sight. Therefore, the term 'blindness' intellectually implies 'sight' because the concept of blindness depends on the concept of sight. This holds even though in reality, blindness exists without sight.
This demonstrates that external coexistence is not required; only the intellectual link matters.
5
Test the crow/blackness counterexample
The passage rejects the claim that 'crow' implies 'blackness' implicitly, because although crows are black externally, the intellect can conceive of a crow that is not black (e.g., red or white). There is no intellectual necessity.
This shows that external concomitance alone is insufficient for implicative signification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا بُدَّ في الدَّلالةِ الالتزاميَّة مِنَ اللُّزُومِ بينَ مُسمَّى اللَّفظِ والخارج. إمَّا عَقْلَاً كاللُّزوم بين الاثنين والزوجية، فإنَّه بحسب العقل. ولا يشترط اللُّزوم الخارجي؛ لأنَّه لو كان شرطاً لم يتحقق الالتزام بدونه، وليس كذلك، فإنَّ العمى يدلُّ على البَصَرِ التزاماً. والحاصل: أنَّهُ لا يشترطُ اللُّزومُ الخارجي زيادةً على الذهني، وأمَّا اللُّزوم الخارجي فقط؛ فعدم كفايتِهِ مُستفاد من اشتراط اللُّزوم الذهني. وحينئذ فلا يُقالُ: إنَّ غراباً يدلُّ على السَّوادِ التزاماً؛ لأنَّهُ وإنْ لزم خارجاً فلا يلزم عقلاً؛ لأنَّ العقل يجوز أن يكونَ الغراب أحمرَ أو أبيض مثلاً.
And it is necessary in implicative signification to have concomitance between the meaning of the term and the external [thing] — either intellectual, like the concomitance between two and evenness, for it is according to the intellect. And external concomitance is not required; because if it were a condition, implicative signification would not occur without it, but it is not so, for blindness signifies sight implicitly. The conclusion: external concomitance is not required in addition to intellectual [concomitance]. As for external concomitance alone, its insufficiency is derived from the requirement of intellectual concomitance. Hence, one cannot say that a crow signifies blackness implicitly, because although it coexists externally, it does not follow intellectually; for the intellect may conceive of a crow being red or white, for example.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage defines the condition for implicative signification (dalala iltizamiyya). The author begins by stating that implicative signification requires a necessary concomitance (luzum) between the term's primary meaning and the external meaning it implies. This concomitance can be intellectual ('aqlan), meaning it is based on conceptual necessity, exemplified by the relationship between 'two' and 'evenness'—whenever the intellect conceives of the number two, it necessarily conceives of it as even. External concomitance (khariji), based on empirical coexistence, is not sufficient. The author argues that if external concomitance were a condition, then implicative signification would fail in cases where the external link is absent but the intellectual link exists. The example of blindness (al-'ama) and sight (al-basar) illustrates this: blindness is defined as the absence of sight, yet the term 'blindness' implicitly signifies sight, even though in reality blindness exists without sight. This shows that the link is intellectual, not external. The conclusion reaffirms that only intellectual concomitance is required; external concomitance alone is insufficient. To demonstrate, the author gives the counterexample of a crow and blackness: a crow is black externally, but there is no intellectual necessity that a crow must be black—one can imagine a white crow. Therefore, one cannot say that 'crow' implicitly signifies blackness. This passage guards against the common misunderstanding that any real-world correlation suffices for implicit signification.

Key Terms
الدلالة الالتزامية
اللزوم
عقلاً
خارجي

B) Because external concomitance never exists in reality.
C) Because the term's primary meaning must be absent.
D) Because implicative signification only applies to opposites.

Key Takeaway
The validity of implicative signification depends on intellectual necessity, not empirical correlation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Reality of Blindness: Simple Absence vs. Conceptual Designation
Commentator refines the definition of blindness using reality vs. concept distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the simple reality (haqiqa basita) and the conceptual designation (mafhum 'unwani) of a term using the example of 'blindness'.

Core Idea
The meaning of a word is its simple reality, not its conceptual description.

In this passage, scholars debate whether 'blindness' includes 'sight' in its definition. The resolution hinges on a crucial distinction: the simple reality of blindness is a pure absence, but its conceptual designation includes the restriction to sight. Understanding this allows 'blindness' to be attributed to 'sight' literally without metaphor. This distinction is essential for precise definition in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial positions
Al-'Attar claims that 'blindness' is placed for absence with restriction to sight but intends absolute absence. Abu al-Fath refutes this: if the restriction were part of the meaning, attributing blindness to sight would require a metaphorical reading; otherwise blindness would mean absolute absence, which is false.
2
Find the key distinction by Mir Zahid
Mir Zahid introduces the difference between 'part of the thing' (juz' al-shay') and 'part of its concept' (juz' mafhumihi). Blindness is a simple quality (absence of sight) in reality; the restriction to sight is part of the concept, not the reality.
This distinction solves the problem: the word is placed for the reality, not the concept.
3
Apply the distinction to the attribution
Since the word 'blindness' is placed for the simple reality (the absence), its attribution to 'sight' is literal because the indication to sight is external to what the word is placed for. No metaphorical removal (tajrid) is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في تعريف الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها. فيكون البصر لازماً للعمى في الذهن مع المعاندة بينهما في الخارج. (قَوْلُهُ: الْمُعَانَدَةِ) أي: المنافاة بينهما في الخارج، وحينئذٍ؛ فَلَا مُلازمةً بينَهُما فيه . العطار لفظ العَمَى الموضوعُ لِلعَدَمِ مَع التَّقييدِ بِالبصرِ ، ويُرادُ مُطلَقُ العدم، ونقض دليلَهُ أبو الفتح بأنَّه لَو تمَّ؛ لَدَلَّ على أن يكونَ التَّقييدُ بالبصر أيضاً خارجاً عَنِ العَمَى؛ لأنَّه لو كانَ دَاخِلاً فيهِ؛ لم يصح إسنادُهُ لِلبَصَرِ بدونِ قرينةٍ مجازيَّةٍ؛ ضرورةً أن المسند إلى البصر هو العدمُ المطلقُ ؛ لا المقيَّدُ بِالبصرِ ، فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ العَمَى عبارةً عَن مُطلَقِ العَدَم ، وهو بَاطِلٌ قَطْعاً، والحقُّ أنَّا لا نسلّمُ صحة إسناده إلى البصر بدونِ قرينة مجازيَّة؛ إذ الأمثلة المذكورة مشتملة على القرينة وهِيَ نَفْسُ إسناده إلى البصر، وأمَّا قَوْلُهُ : والأصلُ الحقيقةُ ؛ فَفيهِ أنَّ الصَّارفَ عَنِ الحقيقةِ مَوجودٌ هَهُنَا ، وهُوَ لُزوم المجازيَّةِ باعتبارِ التَّقييدِ بالبصر ؛ سواء كانَ نَفْسُ البصر داخلاً فيه أو خارجاً عنه ١ هـ وأجاب مير زاهد بأنَّ المسند إلى البصرِ هُوَ نَفْسُ العَمَى، والنِّسبةُ لَيْسَتْ دَاخِلَةٌ فيهِ؛ بَل فيما يعبّر عنه، وإلا ؛ لَكانَ العَمَى أمراً نسبيًّا ، وقد اشتهر بينَهُم الفرقُ بينَ جُزء الشَّيء وجُزءٍ مَفهومه ؛ فالعَمَى صِفَةٌ بسيطةٌ قائمة بالأعمى، وحقيقتُهُ عَدَمٌ خاص يعبر عنهُ بِعَدَم البصر، فالتَّقييدُ بِهِ داخل في هذا المفهوم العنواني وخارج عن حقيقتِهِ البسيطةِ، وَلَمَّا كانَتِ الألفاظ موضوعةً لِلحقائقِ دونَ عنوانها ؛ كانَ دلالةُ العَمَى على البَصَرِ دَلالة على خارج عَنِ الموضوعِ لَهُ ، وكانَ إسناده إليه على سبيل الحقيقة من غَيْرِ تجريدٍ ومَجاز . .ا.هـ. وقال عبد الحكيم : تركُ ذِكْرِ البَصَرِ مَعَهُ فَي نَحْوِ قَولِهِ تعالى: ﴿هُم بُكُمُ عُمّى ﴾ [البقرة: ۱۸]، وفي قوله تعالى : وبَلْ هُم مِّنْهَا عَمُونَ ﴾ [النمل: يدلُّ على دخول البصر في مفهومه، وذِكْرُهُ معه في نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنَّهَا لَا تَعْمَى الْأَبْصَارُ ﴾ [الحج: ٤٦] ؛ يدلُّ على خُروجِهِ عَنْهُ كَي لا يحتاج إلى تجريد ا.هـ. (قَوْلُهُ : مَعَ الْمُعَانَدَةِ بَيْنَهُمَا فِي الْخَارِجِ فَإِنَّهما متقابلانِ تَقابُلَ العَدَمِ والملكة.
Chapter: On defining the three indications and their rulings. So sight is necessary for blindness in the mind, while they are mutually exclusive in external existence. (His statement: 'mutual opposition') meaning: contradiction between them in external existence, so there is no necessary connection between them there. Al-‘Attar: The term 'blindness' is placed for absence with restriction to sight, but what is intended is absolute absence. Abu al-Fath refuted his proof by saying: if it were valid, it would indicate that the restriction to sight is also external to blindness, because if it were included in it, its attribution to sight would not be valid without a metaphorical indicator, necessarily because what is attributed to sight is absolute absence, not the one restricted to sight, so it would follow that 'blindness' means absolute absence, which is false absolutely. The truth is that we do not concede the validity of its attribution to sight without a metaphorical indicator, since the mentioned examples include the indicator, which is the very attribution to sight. As for his statement: 'the default is literal meaning', it contains that the factor preventing literal meaning is present here, which is the necessity of metaphorically depending on the restriction to sight, whether sight itself is included in it or not. Mir Zahid answered: what is attributed to sight is blindness itself; the relation is not included in it, but rather in what is expressed by it; otherwise blindness would be a relative matter. They have famously distinguished between a part of the thing and a part of its concept. So blindness is a simple quality subsisting in the blind person; its reality is a specific absence expressed as 'absence of sight'. So the restriction to it is included in this conceptual designation and external to its simple reality. And since words are placed for realities not for their designations, the indication of 'blindness' to sight is an indication of something external to what it is placed for, and its attribution to it is literal without abstraction or metaphor. ‘Abd al-Hakim said: The omission of the word 'sight' with it in His statement: 'They are deaf, dumb, blind' [Q. 2:18] and in His statement: 'Nay, they are blind from it' [Q. 27:?] indicates that sight is included in its concept. And its mention with it in His statement: 'For indeed the eyes do not become blind' [Q. 22:46] indicates that it is excluded from it so as not to require abstraction. (His statement: 'with mutual opposition between them in external existence—they are opposites in the opposition of absence and possession.')
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hāshiya) refining the definition of the three types of indication, focusing specifically on the relationship between the term 'blindness' (al-‘amā) and 'sight' (al-basar). The author begins by stating that, in the mind, sight is necessary for the concept of blindness, even though in external reality the two are mutually exclusive. The commentary then presents a debate: Al-‘Attar initially suggests that the word 'blindness' is used for absence (al-‘adam) with a restriction to sight, but the intended meaning is absolute absence. Abu al-Fath critiques this, arguing that if the restriction were part of the meaning, then attributing blindness to sight would require a metaphorical reading; otherwise it would imply that blindness is absolute absence, which is false. The truth, according to the response, is that the attribution of 'blindness' to sight is literal because the restriction to sight is part of the conceptual designation (al-mafhūm al-‘unwānī) but not part of the simple reality (al-ḥaqīqa al-basīṭa) of blindness. Mir Zāhid clarifies that the word is placed for the simple reality, not the conceptual designation, so the indication of 'blindness' to sight points to something external to its meaning, allowing literal attribution without metaphor. ‘Abd al-Ḥakīm then provides Quranic evidence: when 'sight' is omitted (as in Q. 2:18), it shows that sight is included in the concept of blindness; when it is mentioned (as in Q. 22:46), it shows that sight is excluded from the concept. The passage concludes by noting that blindness and sight are opposites in the category of 'absence and possession' (taqābul al-‘adam wa al-malaka).

Key Terms
العَمَى
البَصَر
المُعَانَدَة
القَرِينَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Mir Zahid, why is the attribution of 'blindness' to 'sight' literal without metaphor?
A) Because 'blindness' is defined as absolute absence, so it can be directly attributed to sight.
B) Because the restriction to sight is part of the conceptual designation, not the simple reality, so the word is placed for the simple reality.
C) Because the Quranic verses explicitly state that eyes do not become blind.
D) Because the word 'blindness' is a relative term that includes sight by definition.

Key Takeaway
The reality of blindness is a simple absence; its conceptual inclusion of sight is external to its essential meaning, enabling literal attribution without metaphor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Evaluate the definition of 'salat': Some say it means 'du'a' (supplication) literally but is used for the ritual prayer. Using the distinction between simple reality and conceptual designation from this lesson, explain how 'salat' can be attributed to the ritual prayer literally or metaphorically.

Opposition of Absence and Possession: Blindness and Sight
Commentator identifies the type of opposition between blindness and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical category of 'taqabul al-'adam wa al-malaka' and apply it to paired concepts like blindness and sight.

Core Idea
Blindness and sight are opposites in the category of absence and possession, not in the category of contradiction.

The passage concludes by stating that blindness and sight are opposites in the opposition of 'absence and possession'. This is a specific type of opposition distinct from simple contradiction or contrariety. Understanding this category clarifies why they can be both mutually exclusive in external existence yet conceptually linked. In Islamic logic, this classification is crucial for precise reasoning about attributes and their privations.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four types of opposition (taqabul)
In Islamic logic, there are four types: contradiction (naqid), contrariety (tadhad), absence and possession (al-'adam wa al-malaka), and relation (tadayuf). Blindness and sight are an example of the third type.
2
Identify the condition for absence-possession opposition
For this type, the subject must be naturally capable of possessing the attribute. For example, a stone is not called 'blind' because it naturally lacks sight; only a being that should have sight can be described as blind.
This distinguishes it from simple contradiction.
3
Apply to the text's claim
The text says blindness and sight are mutually exclusive in external existence (a blind person cannot see at the same time) but in the mind, sight is necessary for the conception of blindness. This fits the pattern of absence-possession opposition.

What distinguishes the opposition of 'absence and possession' from simple contradiction?
A) In absence-possession, the two opposites can both be true for the same subject.

C) In absence-possession, the opposites are mutually exclusive but not exhaustive.

Customary Necessity is Not Logical Necessity
Commentator rejects customary necessity from logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish customary necessity from rational necessity and justify why customary necessity is excluded from the discipline of logic.

Core Idea
Customary necessity is not logical necessity—logic deals only with rational relations that cannot fail.

In this passage, the commentator sharply rejects the inclusion of customary necessity (al-luzūm al-‘urfī) within the discipline of logic. He argues that only rational necessity (al-luzūm al-‘aqlī) is valid for logical inference. This distinction is crucial for understanding the boundaries of formal logic and why probabilistic associations are not considered reliable in definitive reasoning. The example of rain and plants illustrates how a customary relation can fail in reality, proving it is not logically necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of necessity
Locate in the passage where the commentator distinguishes rational necessity (al-luzūm al-bayyin bi al-ma‘nā al-akhass) from customary necessity (al-luzūm al-‘urfī). Note that rational necessity is self-evident and cannot fail, while customary necessity depends on usual occurrence.
2
Understand the example: rain and plants
The commentator gives the example of the relation between rain (ghayth) and plant growth (nabt). According to custom, rain is followed by plants, so whenever one conceives of rain, one also conceives of plants. However, this is not a rational necessity because exceptions occur.
Think of a drought: rain falls but plants do not grow—this is takhalluf.
3
Grasp the criterion: takhalluf (non-occurrence)
The commentator states that because actual non-occurrence (takhalluf) of the consequent (plants) is possible after the antecedent (rain), the necessity is not rational. The existence of a counterexample proves the relation is contingent, not necessary.
This is a key test: if you can conceive the antecedent without the consequent in reality, it is not a rational necessity.
4
Recognize the commentator's ruling
The commentator declares that considering customary necessity is a departure from the discipline (kharūj ‘an al-fann). Only the evident necessity in the most specific sense (rational) is accepted by the verifiers. Even the broader sense of evident necessity is not considered, let alone customary necessity.
The phrase 'faḍlan' (let alone) is used a fortiori: if the broader sense is rejected, customary necessity is even more strongly rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَوْ عُرْفاً) كاللُّزوم بين الغَيثِ والنَّبتِ، فَإِنَّه بين الغَيثِ والنَّبتِ، فَإِنَّه بحسَبِ العُرفِ لا بالعقل؛ لتحقُّقِ التَّخلُّف. وَاعلَمْ أَنَّ اعتبارَ اللُّزوم العُرفيّ خروج عن الفنّ، فَإِنَّ اللُّزوم المعتبر عند المحققين هو اللُّزومُ البَيِّنُ بالمعنى الأخص كما ذكرنا، وليسَ اللُّزوم البين بالمعنى الأعم معتبراً، فضلاً عن اللزوم العرفي . (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ عُرْفَاً) أي: بأن يمتنع في مجرى العادة تصور الملزوم بدونِ تصورِ اللازم. (قَوْلُهُ : بَيْنَ الْغَيْثِ) أي : المطرِ والنَّبتِ فالغيتُ يلزمُهُ النَّبتُ عُرفاً، فمتى تصوّر الغيث؛ تصوّر النَّبت، ويمتنع بحسب العُرْفِ تصوُّرُ الغيثِ بدونِ تصورِ النَّبت، وقوله : (فإنَّه) ؛ أي: اللازم بين الغيث والنَّبت (قَوْلُهُ : لِتَحَقُّقِ التَّخَلُّفِ أي: تخلف النَّبت عن الغيث، وحينئذ؛ فلا يكونُ اللزوم بينهما عقليًّا (قَوْلُهُ: خُرُوجُ عَنِ الْفَنِّ أي: عن مصطلح الفنّ، وقوله: (كما ذكرنا)؛ أي: في قوله سابقاً : (بل المحققون . . . إلخ). (قَوْلُهُ: هُوَ اللُّزُوْمُ الْبَيْنُ بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَخَصّ) أي: وهو لا يكون إلا عقليا . (قَوْلُهُ: فَضْلاً) هو منصوبٌ على أنَّهُ مفعولٌ مُطلق من فضل بمعنى زاد، وتستعمل بين كلامَيْنِ مُختلفين بالإيجاب والسَّلْبِ، وتقع بعد انتفاء الأدنى؛ ليلزم انتفاء الأعلى بالطَّريقِ الأولى، فالمعنى: وعدم اعتبار اللزوم بالمعنى الأعم أمر
Or customary necessity, like the necessity between rain and plants—it is between rain and plants according to custom, not reason, because actual non-occurrence (of the consequent) is found. Know that considering customary necessity is departure from the discipline. Indeed, the necessity considered by the verifiers is the evident necessity in the most specific sense, as we mentioned, and the evident necessity in the more general sense is not considered, let alone customary necessity. (His statement: 'or customary') meaning: that it is impossible in the course of custom to conceive the antecedent without conceiving the consequent. (His statement: 'between rain') meaning: rain and plants, so rain necessitates plants customarily. Whenever rain is conceived, plants are conceived, and according to custom it is impossible to conceive rain without conceiving plants. And his statement: 'for indeed it is' meaning: the necessity between rain and plants. (His statement: 'because actual non-occurrence exists') meaning: the non-occurrence of plants after rain, so the necessity between them is not rational. (His statement: 'departure from the discipline') meaning: from the terminology of the discipline. And his statement: 'as we mentioned' meaning: in his earlier statement: 'Rather the verifiers... etc.'. (His statement: 'it is the evident necessity in the most specific sense') meaning: and it is only rational. (His statement: 'let alone') is in the accusative as an absolute object from fadala meaning 'increased'. It is used between two contrary statements of affirmation and negation, and occurs after the denial of the lesser to necessitate the denial of the greater a fortiori. So the meaning: and non-consideration of necessity in the more general sense is something
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) rejecting the inclusion of customary necessity (al-luzūm al-‘urfī) in the discipline of logic. The main text mentions that indication can be based on customary necessity, like the association between rain (ghayth) and plant growth (nabt). The commentator, however, sharply critiques this, stating that considering customary necessity is a departure from the discipline (kharūj ‘an al-fann). He explains that the only type of necessity recognized by authoritative scholars (al-muḥaqqiqūn) is rational necessity (al-luzūm al-‘aqlī) in its most specific sense, i.e., the evident conceptual necessity where the necessary connection is self-evident and cannot fail. Even the broader sense of evident necessity (al-luzūm al-bayyin bi al-ma‘nā al-‘a‘amm) is not accepted, let alone customary necessity. The commentator then defines customary necessity: it is when, according to normal custom, the antecedent cannot be conceived without the consequent. For example, rain is customarily followed by plants, so in the mind, one thinks of plants when thinking of rain. But because in external reality plants can fail to appear after rain (taḥaqquq al-takhalluf), the connection is not rational or necessary in the logical sense. The passage ends with a grammatical note on the word 'faḍlan' (let alone), explaining its usage in a fortiori arguments. The overall message is clear: logic deals with rational, necessary, and universal relations, not with customary or probabilistic associations.

Key Terms
اللُّزوم العُرفي
اللُّزوم البَيِّن بالمعنى الأخص
التَّخَلُّف
الفَنّ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say that the necessity between rain and plants is customary rather than rational?
A) Because rain and plants are both mentioned in the Quran.
B) Because plants can fail to appear after rain (takhalluf).
C) Because the relation is based on a syllogism.
D) Because logic does not deal with natural phenomena.

Key Takeaway
Customary necessity, based on habitual association that can fail, is excluded from logic; only rational necessity that cannot fail is accepted.

Customary Concomitance and the Boundaries of Mantiq
Author defines customary necessary concomitance and states it is outside the art of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish customary necessary concomitance from rational necessary concomitance and explain why only the latter is valid in mantiq.

Core Idea
Customary concomitance is not logical proof because separation is possible.

In logic, not every association is a necessary connection. The author illustrates this with the example of rain and vegetation: we expect rain to bring growth, but sometimes it does not. This is customary, not rational. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating when a purported implication is truly logical.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of relation
The passage describes a custom-based relation (luzum 'urfiyy). Note the example: rain and vegetation. What kind of association is this?
The relation is based on frequent observation, not rational necessity.
2
Apply the test of takhalluf
Ask: Can rain occur without vegetation? Yes, sometimes it rains but plants do not grow. This actual separation (takhalluf) proves the relation is not necessary.
If separation is possible, the concomitance is not rational.
3
Compare with rational necessary concomitance
Contrast with 'evident concomitance in the most specific sense' (e.g., contingency implies origination). Here, separation is inconceivable. Only this type is considered within logic.
The author states that even the more general evident concomitance is not considered, let alone customary.
4
Understand the author's verdict
The author declares that considering customary concomitance is a departure from the art (khuruj 'an al-fann). This sets the boundary of mantiq: only rational, separation-proof relations are admitted.
This ruling protects logical proofs from relying on mere empirical regularities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَوْ عُرْفاً كاللُّزوم بين الغيث والنبت، فإنه بحسب العرف لا بالعقل؛ لتحقق التخلف. واعلم أن اعتبار اللزوم العرفي خروج عن الفن، فإن اللزوم المعتبر عند المحققين هو اللزوم البين بالمعنى الأخص كما ذكرنا، وليس اللزوم البين بالمعنى الأعم معتبراً، فضلاً عن اللزوم العرفي.
Or customary: like the concomitance between rain and vegetation, for it is according to custom not reason, because of the actual occurrence of separation. And know that considering customary concomitance is a departure from the art, for the concomitance considered by the verifiers is the evident concomitance in the most specific sense as we mentioned, and the evident concomitance in the more general sense is not considered, let alone customary concomitance.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage defines 'customary necessary concomitance' ('luzum 'urfiyy') and explains why it is not considered a valid logical relation in the discipline of mantiq. The author first gives an example: the concomitance between rain (ghayth) and vegetation (nabt). In everyday experience, rain is followed by vegetation, but this is not a rational necessity; it is merely customary because sometimes it rains and no vegetation grows (takhalluf occurs). Thus, this concomitance is based on custom ('urf) not reason ('aql). The author then asserts that considering such customary concomitance is a departure from the art (khuruj 'an al-fann), meaning the proper domain of logic. The only type of concomitance recognized by the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) is 'evident concomitance in the most specific sense' (al-luzum al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass), which is a rational, necessary connection where the mind cannot separate the two concepts. In contrast, even the 'evident concomitance in the more general sense' (al-luzum al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'am) is not considered valid in logic, so customary concomitance—which is even weaker—is certainly excluded. This distinction is important in the analysis of signification (dalala), where only rational necessary concomitance yields certain knowledge. In Islamic sciences, for example, from 'the world is originated' (hadith) we can rationally infer a creator (khaliq), but from 'rain' we cannot logically infer 'vegetation' because it is only customary. The passage thus sets a boundary for what counts as logical proof.

Key Terms
اللزوم العرفي (al-luzum al-'urfiyy): Necessary concomitance based on custom or probability, where the mind associates two things due to frequent occurrence, but separation is possible in reality. [Note: In later texts on balagha (rhetoric), customary lizum is accepted for metaphorical and metonymic signification, but in pure mantiq it is rejected.]
اللزوم البين بالمعنى الأخص (al-luzum al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass): Evident concomitance in the most specific sense: a rational, necessary relation where the mind cannot conceive the subject without the predicate (e.g., contingency implies origination). [Note: This term is stable across the tradition, but some later logicians broaden it to include some empirical relations.]
التخلف (al-takhalluf): The actual occurrence of separation between two things that are customarily associated, proving the relation is not rational. [Note: Empty string.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider 'customary necessary concomitance' a departure from the art of mantiq?
A) Because it is based on reason, not custom.
B) Because separation between the two things is possible, so it is not a necessary logical relation.
C) Because it is uncertain and therefore belongs to dialectic, not demonstration.
D) Because it involves only empirical observation, which is not part of logic.

Key Takeaway
Only rational necessary concomitance, where separation is impossible, is accepted in mantiq; customary concomitance is excluded because it lacks necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Intention and Conventional Signification
Author states the dispute on irada in dalala wad'iyya and gives the correct view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions on the requirement of intention for conventional signification and state the correct view according to the logicians.

Core Idea
Conventional signification does not require the speaker's intention — the word means what it means regardless.

This passage introduces a key dispute in the theory of signification: does a word only signify its meaning when the speaker intends that meaning? The author presents the weak view (requiring intention) and the accepted view (no intention required). Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how words convey meaning in Islamic disciplines, especially in legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing views
Read the passage and find where the author mentions the dispute: one view stipulates intention (irada) for conventional signification; the other view does not. Mark the phrases that indicate each view.
The phrase 'ishtirat al-irada' indicates the first view.
2
Locate the author's statement of the correct view
Find the sentence beginning with 'wa al-haqq' (and the truth is). This is the author's statement of the accepted position: intention is not a condition.
The correct view is 'adam al-ishtirat'.
3
Understand the role of 'wa law taqdiran'
The phrase 'wa law taqdiran' (even if virtually) is from the base text. Al-Dasuqi explains that this phrase is used to show how implicative and concomitant signification entail correspondence signification on the view that requires intention. Understand that this phrase is a concession to the weak view for the sake of argument.
This phrase does not represent the accepted view.
4
Summarize the implication
Based on the correct view, a word signifies its meaning whenever it is uttered conventionally, regardless of whether the speaker intended that meaning. This principle underlies the reliability of linguistic communication in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في تعريفِ الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها
الدسوقي
وهذا مبنى على أنَّ الدَّلالة الوضعية تتوقَّفُ على الإرادة، وهذا مرجوح عند أهل الفنّ، والمعتبرُ عندَهُم أنَّ اللفظ يدلُّ على معناه الموضوع له؛ سواءٌ حصلت إرادة لهُ أَمْ لَا ، فقوله : (وَلَو تقديراً) ؛ القصد بهذا الإشارة لبيان استلزامِ التَّضمنيَّةِ والالتزامية للمطابقة على مذهب من يشترط الإرادة فى الدَّلالة الوضعية . وحاصل ما في المقامِ أنَّه اختلف في الدَّلالة الوضعية: هل يشترط فيها الإرادة؛ فلا يدلُّ اللَّفْظُ على المعنى إلَّا إذا أريد ذلك المعنى منه؟ ، والحقُّ : عدمُ الاشتراط، وحينئذ فيدلُّ اللَّفْظُ على ما وضعَ له وإن لم ترد منه .
Chapter on the definition of the three significations and their rulings. Al-Dasuqi: And this is based on the fact that conventional signification depends on intention, and this is weak according to the people of the art; and the considered view according to them is that the word signifies its meaning for which it was coined, whether intention for it occurs or not. So his statement: (and even if virtually) – the purpose of this is to indicate the entailment of implicative and concomitant signification for correspondence signification on the view of whoever stipulates intention in conventional signification. And the summary of what is in the discussion is that there is disagreement concerning conventional signification: is intention stipulated in it, so that the word does not signify the meaning unless that meaning is intended? And the truth is: the absence of stipulation, and then the word signifies what it was coined for even if it is not intended.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage begins a new section defining the three types of signification (dalalat) and their rules. Al-Dasuqi first mentions the foundation: some scholars hold that conventional signification (dalala wad'iyya) requires the intention (irada) of the speaker, but this is a weak view (marjuh). The accepted view is that a word signifies its meaning regardless of whether the speaker intended it or not. The phrase "wa law taqdiran" (even if virtually) from the matn is then introduced as an indication of how implicative (tadammun) and concomitant (iltizam) signification entail correspondence (mutabaqa) signification on the view that stipulates intention. The author then summarizes the core issue: the dispute over whether intention is a condition for conventional signification. The correct position (al-haqq) is that intention is not required; the word signifies its meaning even without intention.

Key Terms
الدَّلالة الوضعية
الإرادة
المطابقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Yes, the word signifies its meaning whether intended or not.
D) It depends on the type of signification.

Key Takeaway
The accepted view in logic is that conventional signification is independent of the speaker's intention.

Understanding Direction of Entailment in Significations
Al-Attar explains two interpretations and criticizes a reversal of entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between luzum (necessary concomitance) and istilzam (entailment) and correctly identify the direction of entailment between the three significations (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam).

Core Idea
Direction of entailment matters: mutabaqa entails tadammun and iltizam, not the reverse.

In this passage, al-Attar clarifies an important nuance in the theory of signification. Depending on how one interprets the phrase 'wa law taqdiran', either mutabaqa or luzum is generalized to include virtual cases. He then warns against a misinterpretation that reverses the entailment relationship between the three types of signification. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the logic of definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views on 'wa law taqdiran'
The phrase can be interpreted in two ways: on the first view, 'mutabaqa' is generalized to include actual and virtual; on the second view, 'luzum' is generalized. Note which term is being extended in each view.
2
Locate the criticized interpretation
Some commentators interpret the matn as saying that tadammun and iltizam 'entail' (yastlziman) a virtual mutabaqa. Find this in the Arabic passage.
3
Understand why the interpretation is wrong
Al-Attar explains that 'istilzam' (entailment) is not the same as 'luzum' (necessary concomitance). The matn states 'wa talzamuhuma al-mutabaqa' (and correspondence entails them both), meaning luzum from mutabaqa to tadammun and iltizam. The interpretation reverses this direction. The interpreter (mufassir) must match the interpreted (mufassar).
4
Apply the rule of unified direction
The glossator defends the interpretation by arguing that the interpreter and interpreted must be unified. That is, if the matn says 'X entails Y', then the interpretation cannot say 'Y entails X' without changing the meaning. Check that the interpretation's entailment direction matches the matn's.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العطار
وَلَو كَانَ تَقديريًّا، فَعَلَى الأوَّلِ : المراد بالمطابقة أعمُّ مِنَ التَّحقيقيَّةِ والتَّقديريَّةِ، وعلى الثَّاني : اللُّزوم أعمُّ مِنَ التَّحقيقي والتَّقديري، وعَلَى التَّقديريْنِ : تَفسيرُ الكَلَامِ بأنَّ التَّضمُّنَ والالتزام يستلزمَانِ تقدير المطابقةِ، كَمَا وقع مِن بعض الشَّارِحِينَ ليسَ على ما ينبغي ا.هـ. ووجههُ بعض حواشيه بأنَّ التَّفسير بالاستلزام غير اللزوم في قولِهِ : وَتَلزمهُمَا المطابقة، فاللُّزومُ مِن جانب المطابقة، والاستلزام مِن جانبِ التَّضمُّنِ والالتزامُ، والمفسر والمفسِّرُ يجبُ أنْ يَكُونَا مُتَّحدَيْنِ ; فَافْهَمْ .ا.هـ.
Al-Attar: And it (the phrase) is virtual. So on the first [view], what is meant by correspondence [mutabaqa] is more general than actual and virtual; and on the second [view], the entailment [luzum] is more general than actual and virtual; and on both [views], the interpretation of the statement that implicative and concomitant [signification] entail a virtual correspondence, as occurred by some commentators, is not as it should be. And a glossator justified it by saying that interpretation by entailment (istilzam) is different from entailment (luzum) in his statement: 'and correspondence entails them both' – for luzum is from the side of correspondence, and istilzam is from the side of implicative and concomitant, and the interpreter and the interpreted must be unified. So understand.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by al-Attar on the phrase 'wa law taqdiran' (even if virtually). He explains that depending on which view one adopts regarding intention, the term 'mutabaqa' or 'luzum' is generalized to include both actual and virtual cases. He then criticizes some commentators who interpreted the matn as saying that tadammun and iltizam entail (yastlziman) a virtual mutabaqa. He notes that this interpretation is incorrect because 'istilzam' (entailment) is not the same as 'luzum' (necessary concomitance). The matn says 'wa talzamuhuma al-mutabaqa' (and correspondence entails them both), meaning luzum from correspondence to tadammun and iltizam, whereas the interpretation reverses the direction. The interpreter (mufassir) and the interpreted (mufassar) must be in agreement. He concludes with 'fafham' (so understand).

الاستلزام

Key Takeaway
In logical signification, the direction of entailment must be preserved: mutabaqa entails tadammun and iltizam, not the reverse.

Actual vs Virtual Signification: Abu al-Fath's Clarification
Abu al-Fath explains the intention behind the generalization and the two types of mutabaqa and luzum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between actual and virtual correspondence (mutabaqa) and concomitance (luzum) as defined by Abu al-Fath.

Core Idea
Intention distinguishes actual from virtual signification.

Abu al-Fath unpacks the distinction between actual and virtual types of correspondence and concomitance. He clarifies that the generalization refers to the Shaykh's position on the necessity of intention. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how conventional signification works in Islamic legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two core relations
The passage discusses mutabaqa (correspondence) and luzum (concomitance). Each has an actual (haqiqi) and a virtual (taqdiri) form.
2
Note the role of intention
Actual signification requires intention (irada). Virtual signification is defined hypothetically: if the significand were intended, the signification would be actual.
3
Compare virtual definitions
Virtual mutabaqa: a signification that would be mutabaqa if its significand were intended. Virtual luzum: a concomitance that would be iltizami if its significand were intended.
4
Apply to the Shaykh's two possibilities
The generalization covers both views: intention required only for mutabaqa, or for all conventional signification. The virtual forms accommodate the case where intention is absent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ قال أبو الفتح: والظَّاهِرُ أنَّ هذا التَّعميم إشارة إلى ما ذهب إليهِ الشَّيخُ مِنْ أنَّ الإرادةَ شَرْط في الدَّلالة المطابقيَّة، أو في مُطلَقِ الدَّلالةِ الوضعيَّةِ على الاحتمالين المشهورين في تقرير مذهبه، وإلى توجيه لزومِ التّضمُّنِ والالتزام المطابقة على المذهبين، فالمراد بالمطابقة الحقيقية واللُّزوم الحقيقيّ : حَقيقتُهُمَا، وبالمطابقةِ التَّقديريَّةِ دَلالةٌ لو أُريدَ مَدلولها ; كَانَتْ مُطابقةً، وبِاللُّزومِ التَّقديري : لزوم دلالة لو أُريدَ مَدلُولُهَا; كانت التزاميَّةٌ
Then Abu al-Fath said: And apparent is that this generalization is an indication of what the Shaykh held, that intention is a condition in correspondence signification, or in absolute conventional signification, according to the two famous possibilities in formulating his doctrine; and also an indication of the guidance of the concomitance of implicative and concomitant [signification] to correspondence on the two views. So what is meant by actual correspondence and actual concomitance is their realities, and by virtual correspondence is a signification that, if its significand were intended, would be correspondence; and by virtual concomitance is a concomitance of a signification that, if its significand were intended, would be concomitant.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
Abu al-Fath offers a clear interpretation. He says that the generalization (ta'mim) mentioned earlier points to the Shaykh's (probably Ibn al-Hajib or another authority) position that intention is required either for mutabaqa specifically or for all conventional signification. The generalization also serves to explain how tadammun and iltizam entail mutabaqa on both views (those who require intention and those who do not). He then defines terms: actual mutabaqa and actual luzum refer to the real instances; virtual mutabaqa refers to a signification that would be mutabaqa if its significand were intended; virtual luzum refers to a concomitance that would be iltizami if its significand were intended.

Key Terms
الشيخ
المطابقة الحقيقية
المطابقة التقديرية
اللزوم الحقيقي
اللزوم التقديري

Checkpoint
According to Abu al-Fath, what does 'virtual correspondence' (al-mutabaqa al-taqdiriyya) mean?
A) A signification that is not really correspondence but similar to it.
B) A signification that would be correspondence if its significand were intended.
C) A type of signification that requires no intention.
D) The same as implicative signification.

Key Takeaway
Virtual mutabaqa and virtual luzum are defined hypothetically: they become actual if intention is present, allowing the generalization to cover both views on the role of intention.

Virtual Correspondence: Resolving the Objection about the Verb Without a Subject
Objection and answer about verb without subject and virtual mutabaqa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the concept of virtual correspondence (al-mutabaqa al-taqdiriyya) resolves the objection that a verb without a subject lacks full correspondence.

Core Idea
A verb without a subject has virtual correspondence, preserving its meaning by assumption.

This passage addresses a subtle point about the signification of verbs. When a verb is mentioned without its subject, some argue it lacks full correspondence meaning. The commentator clarifies that it has virtual correspondence, i.e., correspondence on the assumption that the subject is mentioned. This shows how logical analysis can resolve apparent contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text claim
Find the phrase 'wa law taqdiran' in the passage. This is the statement being explained. The commentator is showing how it relates to an objection.
2
Extract the objection
The objection: the verb without its subject does not have actual mutabaqa because its full meaning depends on the subject; it only has tadammun (event and time) and iltizam (a subject). This is the mu'tarid's position.
3
Extract the answer
The answer: the verb does have mutabaqa, but virtual mutabaqa – meaning it has mutabaqa on the assumption that the subject is mentioned. Thus mutabaqa is generalized to include actual and virtual.
4
Recognize the generalization
The term 'mutabaqa' is now used more broadly to include both actual (when subject is present) and virtual (when subject is implied). This resolves the objection without denying the dependence on the subject.
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
In usul al-fiqh, a command like 'aqim al-salat' (establish prayer) implies a subject (the mukallaf). The verb 'establish' has virtual mutabaqa regarding the subject. This tool helps interpret divine commands.
Refer to the 'where_this_tool_lives' section for a detailed example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا مَا قِيلَ في توجيه قولِهِ : وَلَو تقديراً ; أَنَّهُ إشارة إلى خشم سؤالٍ; تقديرُهُ أَنَّ لَفْظَ الفعل بدونِ ذِكْرِ الفاعِل يدلُّ على الحدثِ والزَّمانِ تَضمناً، وَلَا يدلَّ على معناه الموضوع لهُ مُطابقةٌ; لِتوقُفِهِ على ذِكْرِ الفاعل وكَذَا يدلّ على فاعل ما التزاماً بدونِ دَلالة مطابقةٍ، وتقريرُ الجوابِ أنَّ ذِكْرَ الفِعْلِ بِدونِ ذِكْرِ الفاعل ; وإن لم يدلَّ مُطابقةٌ تحقيقاً، لكنَّهُ يدلُّ مُطابقةً تقديراً، بمعنى أنَّهُ يدلّ مُطابقةً على تقديرِ ذِكْرِ الفاعِلِ، والمراد بالمطابقةِ هَهُنَا ; أعمُّ مِنَ التَّحقيقيَّةِ والتَّقديريَّةِ
And as for what was said in explanation of his statement 'wa law taqdiran', that it is an indication of an objection; its formulation is that the word of the verb without mentioning the subject signifies the event and time by implicative signification, and does not signify its meaning for which it was coined by correspondence, because it depends on mentioning the subject; and similarly it signifies some subject by concomitant signification without correspondence. And the formulation of the answer is that the mention of the verb without mentioning the subject, even if it does not signify by actual correspondence, yet it signifies by virtual correspondence, meaning that it signifies by correspondence on the assumption of mentioning the subject; and what is meant by correspondence here is more general than actual and virtual.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the phrase 'wa law taqdiran'. The objection is that a verb like 'qatala' without its subject (like 'Zayd') does not have mutabaqa because its full meaning requires the subject, but it does have tadammun (for event and time) and iltizam (for a subject). The answer is that the verb does have mutabaqa, but virtual mutabaqa, i.e., it would be mutabaqa if the subject were mentioned. Therefore, the term 'mutabaqa' is generalized to include both actual and virtual.

Entailment of Mutabaqa by Tadammun and Iltizam
Al-Dasuqi explains that on both views tadammun and iltizam entail mutabaqa, with the example of fingers in ears.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two views on the entailment of mutabaqa by tadammun and iltizam, using the example of fingers in ears.

Core Idea
Tadammun and iltizam entail mutabaqa only if intention is not stipulated.

The relationship between the three types of signification (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) is a key topic in Islamic logic and usul al-fiqh. Al-Dasuqi clarifies a subtle point: whether tadammun and iltizam always require mutabaqa depends on whether one stipulates that the speaker intends the full meaning. This lesson unpacks that distinction using a concrete Quranic example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
Recall that mutabaqa is the word's indication of its full coined meaning, tadammun indicates a part of that meaning, and iltizam indicates a necessary concomitant. Ensure you are comfortable with these definitions before proceeding.
2
Understand the two views on intention
One view stipulates that the speaker must intend the meaning for signification to occur; the other does not. Note that both views agree that tadammun and iltizam entail mutabaqa, but the nature of that entailment differs.
3
Analyze the entailment on the non-stipulation view
On the view that does not require intention, it is obvious that whenever tadammun or iltizam occur, mutabaqa also occurs. Why? Because the word itself carries all three indications regardless of speaker intent. So the entailment is necessary.
4
Examine the entailment on the stipulation view
On the view that requires intention, it is possible for tadammun or iltizam to occur without mutabaqa if the speaker intends only a part or a necessary concomitant, not the full meaning. The Quranic verse about fingers in ears illustrates this: 'fingers' is used to mean 'some fingers' (tadammun) but the full meaning is not intended, so mutabaqa is absent.
The example shows that tadammun can exist without mutabaqa when intention is stipulated.
5
Apply the distinction to other cases
Consider another example: if someone says 'I saw a lion' meaning a brave man (iltizam), on the stipulation view, iltizam exists but mutabaqa does not because the animal lion is not intended. On the non-stipulation view, both would exist.
This step solidifies understanding through a different example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدسوقي
ثمَّ إِنَّ كُلًّا مِنَ القائل بالاشتراط والقائل بعدمِهِ ; يقولُ باستلزام كلٌّ مِنَ التَّصْمُنيَّةِ والالتزاميَّة للمطابقيَّةِ، لكنَّ الاستلزام ظاهرُ على القول بعدم الاشتراط; إذ لا يوجد التَّضمُّنُ والالتزام في صورة إلا ويوجد فيها المطابقة، وغير ظاهر على القول بالاشتراط ; لأنَّه إذا أريد من اللفظ جزء المعنى أو لازمه كما في: ﴿يَجْعَلُونَ أَصَابِعَهُمْ فِي آذَانِهِمْ ﴾ (البقرة: (۱۹]؛ أي: بعضها ; بقرينة استحالة دخول كلها، ونطقت الحال; أي: دلَّت، فقد وُجِدَ التَّضمُّنُ والالتزام ولم توجد المطابقةُ ; لعدم إرادة الموضوع له، فتنفكُ المطابقة عنهما .
Al-Dasuqi: Then indeed every one of those who stipulate [intention] and those who do not stipulate says that each of implicative and concomitant [signification] entails correspondence; but the entailment is evident on the view of non-stipulation, for there is no case where implicative and concomitant exist except that correspondence exists in it; and it is not evident on the view of stipulation, because if part of the meaning or its necessary concomitant is intended from the word, as in: 'They put their fingers in their ears' (Quran 2:19) – meaning some of them, by the indication of the impossibility of inserting all of them; and 'the state spoke' i.e., indicated; so implicative and concomitant existed and correspondence did not exist, due to the lack of intention of the meaning for which it was coined; so correspondence is separated from them.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
Al-Dasuqi addresses the entailment between the three types of signification. He states that both parties (those who stipulate intention and those who do not) agree that tadammun and iltizam entail mutabaqa. However, the entailment is obvious when intention is not required: whenever tadammun or iltizam occur, mutabaqa also occurs. But when intention is required, it is possible for tadammun or iltizam to occur without mutabaqa, as illustrated by the Quranic verse where 'fingers' is used to mean 'some fingers' (tadammun) or by reading 'the state spoke' as a metonym for indication (iltizam). In such cases, the speaker intends a part or a necessary concomitant, not the full meaning, so mutabaqa is absent.

Key Terms
استلزام
تضمن
التزام
مطابقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, always.
B) No, it is possible for tadammun to exist without mutabaqa, as in the Quranic example.
C) Only if the word is used in a literal sense.
D) Only if the speaker is truthful.

Key Takeaway
The entailment of mutabaqa by tadammun and iltizam depends on whether speaker intention is required; on the stipulation view, they can exist independently, as shown by the fingers-in-ears example.
