The Three Denotations: Mutabaqa, Tadhammun, and Iltizam
Author defines the three denotations with Quranic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the type of denotation (mutabaqa, tadhammun, iltizam) in Quranic and legal expressions.

Core Idea
Correspondence is the primary denotation; implication and concomitance derive from it.

Words signify meanings in three distinct ways: by indicating the full meaning, a part, or a necessary concomitant. This lesson defines these three denotations and illustrates them with Quranic examples, forming the basis for analyzing definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Define correspondence (mutabaqa)
Mutabaqa is when a word denotes its entire subject matter, e.g., 'finger' denotes the whole finger. This is the default denotation set by linguistic convention (wad').
2
Define implication (tadhammun)
Tadhammun is when a word denotes a part of its subject matter. Example: 'fingers' in Q. 2:19 denotes only some fingers, not all, due to impossibility of all fingers entering the ear.
The part is inherent in the whole, so tadhammun is based on a real inclusion relation.
3
Define concomitance (iltizam)
Iltizam is when a word denotes something necessarily implied by its subject matter, e.g., 'the state speaks' denotes that it indicates, because speech necessitates indication.
The concomitant is extrinsic but necessary.
4
Notice the dependency
Both tadhammun and iltizam depend on mutabaqa: if the word had no subject matter, it could not denote a part or concomitant. Thus, the three are not independent.
This dependency is the basis for the later discussion of necessitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في تعريفِ الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها
ثمَّ إِنَّ كُلًّا مِنَ القائل بالاشتراط والقائل بعدمِهِ يَقولُ باستلزام كلٍّ مِنَ التَّضمُّنِيَّةِ والالتزامِيَّةِ للمُطابَقِيَّةِ، لكنَّ الاستلزام ظاهِرٌ على القولِ بعدم الاشتراط؛ إذ لا يُوجَد التَّضمُّنُ والالتزام في صورةٍ إلَّا ويُوجَد فيها المُطابَقَةُ، وغيرُ ظاهِرٍ على القولِ بالاشتراط؛ لأنَّه إذا أُرِيدَ من اللفظ جزؤه أو لازمُه كما في: ﴿يَجْعَلُونَ أَصَابِعَهُمْ فِي آذَانِهِمْ﴾ [البقرة: ١٩] أي: بعضُها؛ بقَرينةِ استحالةِ دخولِ كُلِّها، ونَطَقَتِ الحالُ أي: دَلَّتْ، فقد وُجِدَ التَّضمُّنُ والالتزامُ ولم تُوجَد المُطابَقَةُ؛ لعدمِ إرادةِ الموضوعِ له، فتنفكُ المُطابَقَةُ عنهما.
ووَجَّه المُصَنِّفُ الاستلزامَ على هذا القول بأنَّ الاستلزامَ تَقديريٌ، بمعنى أنَّ كُلَّ لفظٍ له دلالةٌ تضمُّنِيَّةٌ والتزامِيَّةٌ، فهو على تَقديرٍ لو أُرِيدَ منه الموضوعُ له كانَ له دلالةُ مُطابقةٍ. إذا علمتَ هذا فاعلَمْ أنَّ قول المصنِّف: "ويَلزَمُهما المُطابَقَةُ" أي: تَحْقيقاً على القولِ بعدم الاشتراط، وقوله: "ولو تَقديراً" أي: تَقديرَ إرادةِ الموضوعِ له على القولِ بالاشتراط، فيكون المُصنِّفُ بَيَّنَ استلزامَهما للمُطابَقَةِ على القولين، وحينئذٍ فلا يَلزَمُ من قوله "ولو تقديراً" اختيارُ مذهبِ القائلِ بالاشتراط، مع أنَّه أَبطَله في بعضِ كُتُبِهِ.
ضَرَبَ مَثَلًا بدونِ الفاعل على مَعنى إذ لا استعمالَ له بدونِ الفاعل أصلاً، ولو سَلِمَ فنَقولُ: إنَّها مُطابَقِيَّةٌ؛ لأنَّ دلالةَ الفعلِ على الحدثِ بجَوهرِه الموضوعِ له، ودَلالَتَه على النِّسبةِ والزَّمانِ بِهَيئَتِه الموضوعةِ له نَوعيٍ. ا.هـ. وهو كلامٌ حَسَنٌ رافِعٌ للسُّؤالِ من أصلِهِ. وإذا أَمْعَنْتَ النَّظَرَ فيما نَقَلناهُ لكَ؛ تَعلَمُ ما تَلاعَبَ بهِ المُحَشِّيانِ من أَطرافِ الكلامِ، ومِنَ العَجيبِ قولُ بعضِهم في مَقامِ الرَّدِّ على الآخرِ: أنَّ فَهمَ جُزءِ المعنى الموضوعِ لهُ من حيثُ إنَّه جُزءُ تمامِ المعنى الموضوعِ له بدونِ تمامِ فهمِ المعنى الموضوعِ له مُحالٌ، فكيفَ يكونُ جائزاً فَضلاً عن أن يكونَ كثيراً؟! إذ هو فَهمُ الأَخَصِّ من حيثُ كَونُه أَخَصَّ بدونِ فَهمِ الأَعَمِّ. ا.هـ. فَإنَّ كَونَ الجُزءِ أَخَصَّ؛ اشتباهٌ بينَ جُزءِ الشيءِ وفَردِهِ، والفَرقُ بينَهما ظاهِرٌ. وبَعدَ هذا كُلِّهِ؛ فَالأَحسَنُ أنَّ قَولَهُ "وَلَو تَقديراً" إشارةٌ إلى أحدِ أجوبةٍ ثلاثةٍ ذكَرَها المُصَنِّفُ في شَرْحِ الأَصلِ عن سُؤالٍ؛ هو أنَّه إذا أُطلِقَ اللَّفظُ على جُزءِ المعنى أو لازمِه مَجازاً مع قَرينةٍ مانِعَةٍ عن إرادَةِ المعنى الموضوعِ له المُطابَقِيِّ؛ كما هو مَبْنى استعمالاتِ البَيانيِّين، ففي هذه الحالَةِ وُجِدَ التَّضمُّنُ أو الالْتِزامُ بدونِ المُطابَقَةِ؛ فأينَ الاستلزامُ؟ وحاصِلُ الجوابِ أنَّ المُرادَ باستلزامِهما المُطابَقَةَ هو أنَّ كُلَّ لفظٍ له دلالةٌ تضمُّنِيَّةٌ أو التِزامِيَّةٌ فَلَهُ دلالةٌ مُطابَقِيَّةٌ في الجملةِ، وإن لم تُوجَد في تلك.
Chapter: On the definition of the three denotations and their rulings.
Then every one of the proponent of conditionality and the proponent of non-conditionality says that each of the implied and concomitant denotations necessitates the correspondence denotation, but the necessitation is apparent on the view of non-conditionality; because there is no implied or concomitant denotation in any form except that correspondence is found in it, and it is not apparent on the view of conditionality; because if from the word its part or necessary implication is intended, as in: 'They put their fingers in their ears' (al-Baqarah 2:19), i.e., some of them, by the indication of the impossibility of inserting all of them, and 'the state speaks' i.e., indicates, then the implied and concomitant denotations are found and correspondence is not found, due to the non-intention of the subject matter, so correspondence separates from them.
And the author explained the necessitation on this view by saying that the necessitation is suppositional, meaning that every word that has an implied and a concomitant denotation, then on a supposition if its subject matter were intended, it would have a correspondence denotation. If you have understood this, then know that the author's saying: 'And correspondence necessitates them' means actually on the view of non-conditionality, and his saying: 'Even if suppositionally' means supposition of the intention of the subject matter on the view of conditionality, so the author explained the necessitation of both for correspondence on the two views, and then it does not follow from his saying 'even if suppositionally' the choice of the view of the proponent of conditionality, even though he invalidated it in some of his books.
He gave an example without the agent, meaning that there is no usage for it without the agent at all, and if it is sound then we say: It is correspondence, because the denotation of the verb for the event is by its substance that is subject matter for it, and its denotation for the relation and time is by its form that is subject matter for it generically. End quote. And it is a good statement that removes the question from its root. And if you deeply consider what we have quoted for you, you will know what the two commentators played with from the sides of the discourse. And from the amazing is the statement of some of them in the context of refuting the other: That understanding a part of the subject matter by virtue of it being a part of the complete subject matter without complete understanding of the subject matter is impossible, so how can it be permissible, let alone frequent?! For it is understanding the more specific by virtue of it being more specific without understanding the more general. End quote. For the part being more specific is a confusion between a part of a thing and an individual of it, and the difference between them is apparent. And after all this, the best is that his saying 'even if suppositionally' is an indication to one of three answers that the author mentioned in the explanation of the original about a question: That if the word is used for a part of the meaning or its necessary implication metaphorically with an indication preventing the intention of the subject matter that is correspondence, as is the basis of the usages of the rhetoricians, then in this case the implied or concomitant is found without correspondence; so where is the necessitation? And the summary of the answer is that what is meant by the necessitation of both for correspondence is that every word that has an implied or concomitant denotation then it has a correspondence denotation in general, even if it is not found in that particular instance.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) that defines the three types of denotation—correspondence (dalālat muṭābaqa), implication (dalālat taḍammun), and concomitance (dalālat iltizām)—and addresses a key objection regarding their interrelation. The author begins by stating that both proponents of conditionality (ishtirāṭ) and non-conditionality agree that each of implication and concomitance necessitates correspondence. However, the necessitation is obvious on the non-conditionality view: whenever a word is used for a part or necessary meaning, the full meaning is also understood. On the conditionality view, it is not obvious because sometimes a word is used for a part or necessary meaning without intending the full meaning, as in the Quranic verse 'They put their fingers in their ears' (al-Baqarah 2:19), where 'fingers' means 'some fingers' (implication) and not the full meaning. Here, implication is present but correspondence is absent. The author then explains that on the conditionality view, the necessitation is suppositional (taqdīrī): if the full meaning were intended, the word would have correspondence denotation. He clarifies that the author's phrase 'and correspondence necessitates them' means actual necessitation on non-conditionality, and 'even if suppositionally' means by supposition on conditionality, without committing to the conditional view. Next, the commentator offers an alternative answer: the verb's denotation for the event is by its substance, and for relation and time by its form, so it is actually correspondence because the word's own form indicates these meanings. He then criticizes some commentators for confusing a 'part of a thing' with an 'individual of a thing', arguing that understanding a part without the whole is possible, whereas understanding an individual without the genus is impossible. Finally, he concludes that the best answer is that the author's phrase 'even if suppositionally' refers to one of three answers given in the original commentary: in figurative usage where a part or concomitant is intended with a preventing indication, the word still has correspondence denotation in a general sense (fī l-jumla), even if not in that particular usage.

الْتِزام

Key Takeaway
The three denotations are distinguished by whether the word signifies the full meaning, a part, or an extrinsic necessary implication.

The Necessitation of Correspondence: Resolving the Figurative Usage Objection
Author resolves the objection by introducing suppositional necessitation and warns against a common fallacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the concept of suppositional (taqdiri) necessitation resolves the objection that tadhammun and iltizam can occur without mutabaqa in figurative language.

Core Idea
Even in metaphor, tadhammun and iltizam entail mutabaqa either actually or suppositionally.

A key objection to the claim that tadhammun and iltizam necessitate mutabaqa arises from figurative usage: in metaphors like 'the state speaks', the word's full meaning is not intended, yet tadhammun or iltizam occurs. How can mutabaqa be present? This lesson unpacks the logical resolution using the notion of suppositional necessitation.

Guided Path
1
State the necessitation claim
Both proponents of conditionality and non-conditionality agree that tadhammun and iltizam necessitate mutabaqa, i.e., whenever a word has a part or concomitant meaning, it also has a correspondence meaning.
This is a logical relation between the types of denotation.
2
Identify the objection
On the conditionality view, a word used metaphorically (e.g., 'fingers' for some fingers) has tadhammun but not mutabaqa, because the full meaning is not intended. Thus, the necessitation seems false.
The objector gives the examples from Q. 2:19 and 'the state speaks'.
3
Analyze the resolution: suppositional necessitation
The author explains that the necessitation on the conditionality view is suppositional (taqdiri): if the full meaning were intended, the word would have mutabaqa. In other words, the word retains the capacity for mutabaqa even when not actual.
This preserves the logical relation without requiring actual correspondence in every usage.
4
Consider an alternative answer from the commentator
The commentator adds that in the case of verbs like 'speaks', the denotation for the event (speaking) is by the word's substance (mutabaqa), and the denotation for relation and time is by the word's form, also mutabaqa. So the verb actually has mutabaqa for multiple meanings, not just suppositionally.
This suggests that some apparent cases of iltizam are actually mutabaqa when the word's full wad' is considered.
5
Identify the fallacy of confusing part and individual
The commentator warns against the mistaken objection that understanding a part without the whole is impossible, because a part is not the same as an individual of a genus. Understanding a part (e.g., 'some fingers') is possible without the whole, whereas understanding a specific individual (e.g., 'dog') requires the genus ('animal') because it is a species of that genus.
This fallacy arises from confusing a 'part of a thing' with an 'individual of a genus'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في تعريفِ الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها
ثمَّ إِنَّ كُلًّا مِنَ القائل بالاشتراط والقائل بعدمِهِ يَقولُ باستلزام كلٍّ مِنَ التَّضمُّنِيَّةِ والالتزامِيَّةِ للمُطابَقِيَّةِ، لكنَّ الاستلزام ظاهِرٌ على القولِ بعدم الاشتراط؛ إذ لا يُوجَد التَّضمُّنُ والالتزام في صورةٍ إلَّا ويُوجَد فيها المُطابَقَةُ، وغيرُ ظاهِرٍ على القولِ بالاشتراط؛ لأنَّه إذا أُرِيدَ من اللفظ جزؤه أو لازمُه كما في: ﴿يَجْعَلُونَ أَصَابِعَهُمْ فِي آذَانِهِمْ﴾ [البقرة: ١٩] أي: بعضُها؛ بقَرينةِ استحالةِ دخولِ كُلِّها، ونَطَقَتِ الحالُ أي: دَلَّتْ، فقد وُجِدَ التَّضمُّنُ والالتزامُ ولم تُوجَد المُطابَقَةُ؛ لعدمِ إرادةِ الموضوعِ له، فتنفكُ المُطابَقَةُ عنهما.
ووَجَّه المُصَنِّفُ الاستلزامَ على هذا القول بأنَّ الاستلزامَ تَقديريٌ، بمعنى أنَّ كُلَّ لفظٍ له دلالةٌ تضمُّنِيَّةٌ والتزامِيَّةٌ، فهو على تَقديرٍ لو أُرِيدَ منه الموضوعُ له كانَ له دلالةُ مُطابقةٍ. إذا علمتَ هذا فاعلَمْ أنَّ قول المصنِّف: "ويَلزَمُهما المُطابَقَةُ" أي: تَحْقيقاً على القولِ بعدم الاشتراط، وقوله: "ولو تَقديراً" أي: تَقديرَ إرادةِ الموضوعِ له على القولِ بالاشتراط، فيكون المُصنِّفُ بَيَّنَ استلزامَهما للمُطابَقَةِ على القولين، وحينئذٍ فلا يَلزَمُ من قوله "ولو تقديراً" اختيارُ مذهبِ القائلِ بالاشتراط، مع أنَّه أَبطَله في بعضِ كُتُبِهِ.
ضَرَبَ مَثَلًا بدونِ الفاعل على مَعنى إذ لا استعمالَ له بدونِ الفاعل أصلاً، ولو سَلِمَ فنَقولُ: إنَّها مُطابَقِيَّةٌ؛ لأنَّ دلالةَ الفعلِ على الحدثِ بجَوهرِه الموضوعِ له، ودَلالَتَه على النِّسبةِ والزَّمانِ بِهَيئَتِه الموضوعةِ له نَوعيٍ. ا.هـ. وهو كلامٌ حَسَنٌ رافِعٌ للسُّؤالِ من أصلِهِ. وإذا أَمْعَنْتَ النَّظَرَ فيما نَقَلناهُ لكَ؛ تَعلَمُ ما تَلاعَبَ بهِ المُحَشِّيانِ من أَطرافِ الكلامِ، ومِنَ العَجيبِ قولُ بعضِهم في مَقامِ الرَّدِّ على الآخرِ: أنَّ فَهمَ جُزءِ المعنى الموضوعِ لهُ من حيثُ إنَّه جُزءُ تمامِ المعنى الموضوعِ له بدونِ تمامِ فهمِ المعنى الموضوعِ له مُحالٌ، فكيفَ يكونُ جائزاً فَضلاً عن أن يكونَ كثيراً؟! إذ هو فَهمُ الأَخَصِّ من حيثُ كَونُه أَخَصَّ بدونِ فَهمِ الأَعَمِّ. ا.هـ. فَإنَّ كَونَ الجُزءِ أَخَصَّ؛ اشتباهٌ بينَ جُزءِ الشيءِ وفَردِهِ، والفَرقُ بينَهما ظاهِرٌ. وبَعدَ هذا كُلِّهِ؛ فَالأَحسَنُ أنَّ قَولَهُ "وَلَو تَقديراً" إشارةٌ إلى أحدِ أجوبةٍ ثلاثةٍ ذكَرَها المُصَنِّفُ في شَرْحِ الأَصلِ عن سُؤالٍ؛ هو أنَّه إذا أُطلِقَ اللَّفظُ على جُزءِ المعنى أو لازمِه مَجازاً مع قَرينةٍ مانِعَةٍ عن إرادَةِ المعنى الموضوعِ له المُطابَقِيِّ؛ كما هو مَبْنى استعمالاتِ البَيانيِّين، ففي هذه الحالَةِ وُجِدَ التَّضمُّنُ أو الالْتِزامُ بدونِ المُطابَقَةِ؛ فأينَ الاستلزامُ؟ وحاصِلُ الجوابِ أنَّ المُرادَ باستلزامِهما المُطابَقَةَ هو أنَّ كُلَّ لفظٍ له دلالةٌ تضمُّنِيَّةٌ أو التِزامِيَّةٌ فَلَهُ دلالةٌ مُطابَقِيَّةٌ في الجملةِ، وإن لم تُوجَد في تلك.
Chapter: On the definition of the three denotations and their rulings.
Then every one of the proponent of conditionality and the proponent of non-conditionality says that each of the implied and concomitant denotations necessitates the correspondence denotation, but the necessitation is apparent on the view of non-conditionality; because there is no implied or concomitant denotation in any form except that correspondence is found in it, and it is not apparent on the view of conditionality; because if from the word its part or necessary implication is intended, as in: 'They put their fingers in their ears' (al-Baqarah 2:19), i.e., some of them, by the indication of the impossibility of inserting all of them, and 'the state speaks' i.e., indicates, then the implied and concomitant denotations are found and correspondence is not found, due to the non-intention of the subject matter, so correspondence separates from them.
And the author explained the necessitation on this view by saying that the necessitation is suppositional, meaning that every word that has an implied and a concomitant denotation, then on a supposition if its subject matter were intended, it would have a correspondence denotation. If you have understood this, then know that the author's saying: 'And correspondence necessitates them' means actually on the view of non-conditionality, and his saying: 'Even if suppositionally' means supposition of the intention of the subject matter on the view of conditionality, so the author explained the necessitation of both for correspondence on the two views, and then it does not follow from his saying 'even if suppositionally' the choice of the view of the proponent of conditionality, even though he invalidated it in some of his books.
He gave an example without the agent, meaning that there is no usage for it without the agent at all, and if it is sound then we say: It is correspondence, because the denotation of the verb for the event is by its substance that is subject matter for it, and its denotation for the relation and time is by its form that is subject matter for it generically. End quote. And it is a good statement that removes the question from its root. And if you deeply consider what we have quoted for you, you will know what the two commentators played with from the sides of the discourse. And from the amazing is the statement of some of them in the context of refuting the other: That understanding a part of the subject matter by virtue of it being a part of the complete subject matter without complete understanding of the subject matter is impossible, so how can it be permissible, let alone frequent?! For it is understanding the more specific by virtue of it being more specific without understanding the more general. End quote. For the part being more specific is a confusion between a part of a thing and an individual of it, and the difference between them is apparent. And after all this, the best is that his saying 'even if suppositionally' is an indication to one of three answers that the author mentioned in the explanation of the original about a question: That if the word is used for a part of the meaning or its necessary implication metaphorically with an indication preventing the intention of the subject matter that is correspondence, as is the basis of the usages of the rhetoricians, then in this case the implied or concomitant is found without correspondence; so where is the necessitation? And the summary of the answer is that what is meant by the necessitation of both for correspondence is that every word that has an implied or concomitant denotation then it has a correspondence denotation in general, even if it is not found in that particular instance.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) that defines the three types of denotation—correspondence (dalālat muṭābaqa), implication (dalālat taḍammun), and concomitance (dalālat iltizām)—and addresses a key objection regarding their interrelation. The author begins by stating that both proponents of conditionality (ishtirāṭ) and non-conditionality agree that each of implication and concomitance necessitates correspondence. However, the necessitation is obvious on the non-conditionality view: whenever a word is used for a part or necessary meaning, the full meaning is also understood. On the conditionality view, it is not obvious because sometimes a word is used for a part or necessary meaning without intending the full meaning, as in the Quranic verse 'They put their fingers in their ears' (al-Baqarah 2:19), where 'fingers' means 'some fingers' (implication) and not the full meaning. Here, implication is present but correspondence is absent. The author then explains that on the conditionality view, the necessitation is suppositional (taqdīrī): if the full meaning were intended, the word would have correspondence denotation. He clarifies that the author's phrase 'and correspondence necessitates them' means actual necessitation on non-conditionality, and 'even if suppositionally' means by supposition on conditionality, without committing to the conditional view. Next, the commentator offers an alternative answer: the verb's denotation for the event is by its substance, and for relation and time by its form, so it is actually correspondence because the word's own form indicates these meanings. He then criticizes some commentators for confusing a 'part of a thing' with an 'individual of a thing', arguing that understanding a part without the whole is possible, whereas understanding an individual without the genus is impossible. Finally, he concludes that the best answer is that the author's phrase 'even if suppositionally' refers to one of three answers given in the original commentary: in figurative usage where a part or concomitant is intended with a preventing indication, the word still has correspondence denotation in a general sense (fī l-jumla), even if not in that particular usage.

Key Takeaway
The logical relation of necessitation between tadhammun/iltizam and mutabaqa holds universally, but the actual presence of mutabaqa in a given usage depends on context; suppositional necessitation preserves the relation where actual mutabaqa is absent.

Two Interpretations of Mutabaqa: Tahqiqan and Taqdiran
Commentary on the two interpretations of the author's phrase 'ويلزمهما المطابقة'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the 'tahqiqan' and 'taqdiran' interpretations of mutabaqa and explain why the author's wording does not imply personal endorsement of the conditional view.

Core Idea
One phrase can accommodate two opposing views without committing to either — a sign of precise logical writing.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a subtle phrase from the author: what does it mean that mutabaqa is necessary 'even if hypothetically'? He shows that the author covers both opinions in the field, without picking sides. This teaches us how to read a text that aims for comprehensive coverage.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two key phrases
In the Arabic, find the words 'تحقيقاً' and 'ولو تقديراً'. These mark the two interpretations the author is providing.
The word 'ولو' signals a hypothetical scenario.
2
Match each interpretation to a view
'تحقيقاً' corresponds to the view that does not require intention of the literal meaning for mutabaqa to occur. 'تقديراً' corresponds to the view that does require that intention, making mutabaqa only hypothetical.
These two views are known in the science of dalalat.
3
Understand the hypothetical mode
Under the conditional view, mutabaqa is said to be 'hypothetical' because if the speaker intends a non-literal meaning, the direct correspondence is not actual; we only assume it for the sake of argument.
4
Recognize the author's neutrality
The commentator explicitly states that mentioning the hypothetical interpretation does not mean the author endorses the conditional view, especially since he refuted it elsewhere.
This is a critical refinement: the author is being inclusive, not taking sides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Thus the author explained the necessity of mutabaqa for both according to the two opinions. In that case, it does not follow from his saying 'even if hypothetically' that he chooses the doctrine of the one who stipulates condition, despite the fact that he refuted it in some of his books.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's phrase 'ويلزمهما المطابقة'. The commentator clarifies that the author has two interpretations: one for each opinion (with and without condition). The phrase 'ولو تقديراً' is used to accommodate the view that requires intention of the literal meaning for mutabaqa to be actual; in that view, mutabaqa is present only hypothetically. The commentator emphasizes that this does not mean the author himself adopts that view, since he refuted it elsewhere.

The Dependence of Tadammun and Iltizam on Mutabaqa
Answering the objection that tadammun and iltizam can occur without mutabaqa in metaphor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the logical relationship between tadammun, iltizam, and mutabaqa in metaphorical indication, and reconstruct the categorical syllogism used to defend their interdependence.

Core Idea
Metaphorical indication is mutabaqa by type-coinage; tadammun and iltizam logically depend on mutabaqa.

This passage addresses a critical objection: In metaphor, tadammun and iltizam seem to exist without mutabaqa because the literal meaning is not intended. The commentator offers two key answers—first, that mutabaqa is present in a general sense; second, that metaphorical indication is itself mutabaqa due to type-coinage. The passage concludes with a categorical syllogism that formalizes the subordination of tadammun and iltizam to mutabaqa. We will walk through these arguments step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the challenger's claim: that in metaphor, tadammun or iltizam occur without mutabaqa because the qarina prevents the literal meaning. Why is this a problem for the logician?
The objection targets the necessity (istilzam) of mutabaqa for the other two types.
2
Examine the first answer
The commentator says mutabaqa is still present 'in general' (في الجملة). This means every word capable of tadammun or iltizam also has a mutabaqa indication somewhere, even if not in the specific metaphorical usage.
This answer maintains the universal claim of necessity.
3
Examine the second answer
The second answer reclassifies metaphorical indication as mutabaqa, not tadammun or iltizam. Why? Because the word is coined for its metaphorical meaning by type (وضع نوعي). Thus the word directly indicates the metaphorical meaning.
This is a stronger answer: it denies that metaphor involves tadammun/iltizam at all.
4
Reconstruct the categorical syllogism
The passage ends with a syllogism: Major premise: Tadammun and iltizam are subordinate (تابع) to mutabaqa. Minor premise: A subordinate cannot exist without its superior. Conclusion: Tadammun and iltizam cannot exist without mutabaqa. Write this in standard categorical form.
This syllogism formalizes the logical dependency regardless of the specific metaphorical case.

Source Text

فالأحسن أن قوله ولو تقديراً إشارة إلى أحد أجوبة ثلاثة ذكرها المصنف في شرح الأصل عن سؤال هو أنه إذا أطلق اللفظ على جزء المعنى أو لازمه مجازاً مع قرينة مانعة عن إرادة المعنى الموضوع له المطابقي كما هو مبنى استعمالات البيانيين ففي هذه الحالة وجد التضمن أو الالتزام بدون المطابقة فأين الاستلزام؟ وحاصل الجواب أن المراد باستلزامهما المطابقة هو أن كل لفظ له دلالة تضمنية أو التزامية فله دلالة مطابقية في الجملة وإن لم توجد في تلك الحالة. الحالة الثانية من تلك الأجوبة منع كون دلالة المجاز على معناه تضمناً أو التزاماً بل هي مطابقة فالمراد بالوضع في تعريف الدلالة أعم من الجزئي الشخصي كما في المفردات والكلي النوعي كما في المركبات وإلا لبقيت دلالة المركبات خارجاً عن الأقسام والمجاز موضوع بإزاء معناه المجازي بالنوع على ما تقرر في موضعه فدلالته عليه مطابقة لأنها دلالة اللفظ على ما وضع بالنوع والتضمن إنما هو فهم الجزء في ضمن الكل والالتزام فهم اللازم مع الملزوم وتبعيته. وإنما نقلنا الجواب الثاني وإن كان لا يخصنا هنا إلا أنه يندفع به ما قد يتوهم من إشكال كون دلالة اللفظ على معناه المجازي مطابقة كما صرح به في كثير من كتب هذا الفن مع أن أهل البيان يجعلون المجاز والكناية متفرعين على هاتين الدلالتين تأمل. قوله لأنه تابعان فيه قياس اقتراني هكذا التضمن والالتزام تابعان للمطابقة والتابع من حيث هو تابع لا يوجد بدون متبوعه ينتج التضمن والالتزام لا يوجدان بدونها.
The best view is that his saying 'and even if hypothetically' refers to one of three answers mentioned by the author in his commentary on the original text to a question, which is: when a word is applied metaphorically to part of the meaning or its concomitant with a qarina preventing the intention of the literal direct meaning, as is the basis of usage by scholars of rhetoric, then in this case tadammun or iltizam is found without mutabaqa: so where is the necessity? The summary of the answer is that what is meant by the necessity of mutabaqa for both of them is that every word that has a tadammun or iltizam indication has a mutabaqa indication overall, even if it is not found in that particular case. The second of those answers: the denial that the metaphorical indication of a word on its metaphorical meaning is tadammun or iltizam; rather it is mutabaqa. So what is meant by وضع (coinage) in the definition of indication is more general: it includes the particular individual coinage as in simple words, and the generic type coinage as in compounds; otherwise the indication of compounds would remain outside the divisions. And metaphor is coined for its metaphorical meaning by type, as established in its place. So its indication upon it is mutabaqa, because it is the indication of a word on what it is coined for by type. Tadammun is only understanding the part within the whole, and iltizam is understanding the concomitant with the accompanied and its subordination. We have transmitted the second answer even though it is not specific to our context, only that it repels what might be imagined as an objection about the fact that the indication of a word for its metaphorical meaning is mutabaqa, as stated in many books of this art, even though the scholars of rhetoric make metaphor and metonymy derived from these two indications. Reflect. His saying 'because they are subordinate' contains a categorical syllogism as follows: tadammun and iltizam are subordinate to mutabaqa, and that which is subordinate as subordinate does not exist without its superior, therefore tadammun and iltizam do not exist without it.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents a question: In metaphorical usage, tadammun and iltizam seem to occur without mutabaqa because the literal meaning is not intended. The answer is twofold: first, that mutabaqa is still present in a general sense (the word has mutabaqa somewhere, even if not in this specific utterance). Second, that metaphorical indication itself is mutabaqa, not tadammun or iltizam, because metaphor is coined for its metaphorical meaning by type. The commentator explains that the 'وضع' in definition of indication is generic, covering both particular and universal coinage, so the metaphorical meaning is directly indicated. The syllogism at the end analyzes the relationship: tadammun and iltizam are subordinate to mutabaqa, so they cannot exist without it.

Basic Division of Coined Words: Compound vs Simple
Commentator defines compound expression and distinguishes it from simple

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between compound (murakkab) and simple (mufrad) expressions based on the commentary's criteria.

Core Idea
A coined word is either compound with intended parts arranged in hearing, or simple without.

The matn gives the foundational division of all coined words that indicate by correspondence. The commentary expands on what makes a word compound, emphasizing intention and auditory arrangement. This lesson will help you apply this division to Arabic terms found in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Locate the matn's dichotomy
The matn says: 'The coined word... is either compound or simple.' Note that the division only applies to words that are coined (mawḍū') and indicate by correspondence.
2
Understand the definition of compound
According to the commentary, compound is 'if a part of it is intended' meaning the part arranged in hearing. The word has parts, each intended to indicate part of the meaning, and those parts are perceived sequentially in audition.
3
Contrast with simple
Simple words have no parts that are intended to indicate parts of the meaning, or such parts are not arranged in hearing. Example: 'insān' is simple (the whole word indicates the whole meaning), while 'rāmī al-ḥijārah' is compound (parts indicate parts).
4
Check exclusions
The commentary lists exclusions: non-verbal, meaningless, mixtures, synonyms, apposition/emphasis – these are not part of the division because they are not truly compound or simple in the logical sense.

Chapter: On Investigations of Words. The coined word (al-lafz al-mawdu') for the meaning by correspondence (muṭābaqah) is either compound or simple. Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'and the coined word' – he restricted to 'word' rather than 'signifier' because it is what is described as compound or simple, and because the discussion is about the indication of words. His statement: 'the coined' meaning: a personal coinage or a generic one like metaphor. Al-'Attar: His statement: 'the coined for the meaning' – that is by coining the very word for the very meaning, as in the coinage of 'insān' for the rational animal; whether the coinage is personal like the example, or generic as in derived terms, or by coining parts for parts as in 'rāmī al-ḥijārah' and 'Zaydun qā'imun'; for the first part is coined for a meaning, and the second part for another meaning, so when the two meanings are taken together, the total word is coined for the total meaning; it is not a coinage of the very word for the very meaning, but a coinage of its parts for its parts. So the compound, from the perspective of composition, has a coinage by virtue of which it enters into the signifier by correspondence, and it is the coinage of its parts for its meaning. As for the generic coinage of the compound by virtue of the form, it has no entry into composition and simplicity, for what is considered therein are the parts arranged in hearing. Thus the division excludes non-verbal coinages, meaningless words, and the compound of a meaningful and meaningless word, for that is not a compound; rather it is a combination of a meaningless word with a used one. Also excluded are two synonyms, because each one indicates what the other indicates; and the explanatory apposition with its object, and the verbal emphasis like 'Zaydun Zaydun' and 'I read the book chapter by chapter', due to the lack of composition; for what is mentioned in terms of meaning, the composition is only in terms of the word for the purpose of emphasis or detail or... (1) (His statement: 'a personal coinage... etc.') The difference between personal and generic coinage is that the first intends the specification of the coined thing, whether the coined-for is specific like Zayd or universal like insān. The second does not intend the specification of the coined thing, but rather the coiner intends a universal matter under which many things fall, like saying: I have coined every word on the pattern of fā'il or maf'ūl to indicate an entity from which or to which the event occurred, and under that rule fall ḍārib, maḍrūb, qātil, maqtūl, and so on. End quote from al-Sharnubi. [Page 236] Definition of compound expression: because it is (if a part of it is intended). Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'and the coined, if intended... etc.' He proceeded here accordingly, and you are aware that there is no need to consider intention here after considering it in the principle of indication. For if you say: Where was it considered in the principle of indication? I say: in his statement: (even if by estimation as we explained). (His statement: 'for the meaning' is attached to 'the coined'. (His statement: 'by correspondence' – the bā' is for association, i.e., a coinage associated with correspondence or meaning, so that it indicates by correspondence. (His statement: 'if a part of it is intended' i.e., the part arranged in hearing. Thus excludes something like 'ḍaraba' because it does not have such a part, since there is no arrangement between the root and the pattern because they are heard simultaneously. So the verb alone is not of the category of compound, but of the category of simple, even if it has two parts: one intended to indicate the event, and the other intended to indicate the time. Al-'Attar: Clarification. End quote. A summary from al-Sayyid and 'Abd al-Ḥakīm with an addition: And when it is clear that these forms are excluded from the divided, they do not fall under either division, even if it is correct that they fall under the simple division by considering each part individually, but the discussion here is about the total of the two words; ponder. (His statement: 'if intended') – in al-Jalāl, there is no need to consider intention here after considering it in the principle of indication, and therefore the Shaykh said: it is needed for understanding, not for completion, end quote. And the author said in the commentary on the original: if by intention is meant actual intention, then compounds before their use and intention of their meanings enter into the definition of simple and exit from the definition of compound. But if by it is meant that it is such that its parts are intended to indicate part of the meaning, then it is compound; otherwise it is simple. So like 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq' as a proper noun, it exits from the definition of simple and enters the definition of compound, because it is such that its two parts are intended to indicate the concept of animal and rational, which are two parts of the person named by it, when applied to a human. And whatever the case, the two definitions are violated both in inclusion and exclusion. End quote. And 'Abd al-Ḥakīm answered that the word is only subject to composition...
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn that divides coined words into compound and simple. The matn states: 'The coined word for the meaning by correspondence is either compound or simple.' The commentator (al-Dasuqi) explains that the matn restricts to 'word' rather than 'signifier' because it is the word that is described as compound or simple, and the discussion is about the indication of words. He clarifies that 'subject' (mawḍū') means either personal coinage (like proper nouns) or generic coinage (like derived terms and metaphors). Al-'Attar then expands on the meaning of 'coined for the meaning'. He explains that coinage can be of three types: (1) the very word for the very meaning (personal or generic), (2) parts for parts (as in compound expressions like 'rāmī al-ḥijārah' where each part has its own meaning), and (3) generic coinage of the compound by form (which is not relevant to the division into compound/simple because that division depends on the parts being arranged in hearing). He then lists what is excluded from the division: non-verbal coinages, meaningless words, combinations of meaningful and meaningless words (which are not truly compound), synonyms (because they do not have parts indicating different meanings), and apposition/emphasis (because they lack composition in the sense of parts contributing to a single meaning). A footnote from al-Sharnubi clarifies the difference between personal and generic coinage. On page 236, the definition of compound is refined: 'if a part of it is intended' meaning the part arranged in hearing. This excludes verbs like 'ḍaraba' because its root and pattern are heard simultaneously and are not arranged in time. Even though the verb has two parts (root and pattern), it is considered simple because the parts are not sequential in hearing. The commentator then discusses the issue of intention, noting that some scholars argue that intention is already considered in the original definition of indication, so it is redundant here. Others differentiate between actual intention and potential intention; if a compound like 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq' used as a proper noun is considered compound because its parts are intended to indicate parts of the meaning when applied to a human. The passage ends with an objection and answer by 'Abd al-Ḥakīm, though it is cut off.

Key Takeaway
The division of coined words into compound and simple hinges on whether the parts are intended to indicate parts of the meaning and are arranged in hearing.

Conditions of Compound Expressions: Intention and Auditory Arrangement
Commentator defines compound expression and distinguishes it from simple

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a given expression meets the conditions for being compound according to the commentary, using the criteria of qaṣd (intention) and tarattub fī al-sam' (arrangement in hearing).

Core Idea
A compound expression requires parts intended to indicate parts of the meaning, sequentially heard.

The commentary delves deeper into what makes a word compound, emphasizing that the parts must be intended (either actually or potentially) and must be arranged in hearing. This lesson will guide you through applying these conditions to distinguish genuine compounds from apparent ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
From the text: (1) the parts are intended to indicate part of the meaning (qaṣd), and (2) the parts are arranged in hearing (mutarattib fī al-sam').
2
Understand the intention condition
The commentary debates whether intention is actual or potential. It says: 'if it is such that its parts are intended...' meaning it is capable of being intended. For example, 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq' as a proper noun is compound because its parts can indicate meaning.
3
Apply the auditory arrangement condition
The verb 'ḍaraba' fails because its parts (root and pattern) are not arranged sequentially; they are heard together. In contrast, 'rāmī al-ḥijārah' has parts arranged in hearing: first 'rāmī', then 'al-ḥijārah'.
4
Test with examples from the text
Consider 'Zaydun qā'imun' (Zayd is standing). The parts are 'Zaydun' and 'qā'imun', each intended to indicate part of the meaning (subject and predicate), and they are arranged in hearing. So it is compound. But synonyms like 'Zaydun wa-ʿAmrun' are not compound because the parts do not combine to form a single meaning.

الإيضاح .ا.هـ. الخَصَ من السَّيِّدِ وعبد الحكيم مع زيادة: وإذا تبيَّن خروج هذهِ الصُّوَرِ عن المقسَّم ؛ فَليسَتْ دَاخلةٌ تحتَ قِسم منهما ، وإن صح دُخولُها في قِسْم المفردِ باعتبارِ كُلّ جزء على حِدَتِهِ، لكنَّ الكلام هنا إنَّما هُوَ في مجموع اللَّفْظَيْنِ ؛ تَأمَّلْ . Chapter: On Investigations of Words. The coined word (al-lafz al-mawdu') for the meaning by correspondence (muṭābaqah) is either compound or simple. Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'and the coined word' – he restricted to 'word' rather than 'signifier' because it is what is described as compound or simple, and because the discussion is about the indication of words. His statement: 'the coined' meaning: a personal coinage or a generic one like metaphor. Al-'Attar: His statement: 'the coined for the meaning' – that is by coining the very word for the very meaning, as in the coinage of 'insān' for the rational animal; whether the coinage is personal like the example, or generic as in derived terms, or by coining parts for parts as in 'rāmī al-ḥijārah' and 'Zaydun qā'imun'; for the first part is coined for a meaning, and the second part for another meaning, so when the two meanings are taken together, the total word is coined for the total meaning; it is not a coinage of the very word for the very meaning, but a coinage of its parts for its parts. So the compound, from the perspective of composition, has a coinage by virtue of which it enters into the signifier by correspondence, and it is the coinage of its parts for its meaning. As for the generic coinage of the compound by virtue of the form, it has no entry into composition and simplicity, for what is considered therein are the parts arranged in hearing. Thus the division excludes non-verbal coinages, meaningless words, and the compound of a meaningful and meaningless word, for that is not a compound; rather it is a combination of a meaningless word with a used one. Also excluded are two synonyms, because each one indicates what the other indicates; and the explanatory apposition with its object, and the verbal emphasis like 'Zaydun Zaydun' and 'I read the book chapter by chapter', due to the lack of composition; for what is mentioned in terms of meaning, the composition is only in terms of the word for the purpose of emphasis or detail or... (1) (His statement: 'a personal coinage... etc.') The difference between personal and generic coinage is that the first intends the specification of the coined thing, whether the coined-for is specific like Zayd or universal like insān. The second does not intend the specification of the coined thing, but rather the coiner intends a universal matter under which many things fall, like saying: I have coined every word on the pattern of fā'il or maf'ūl to indicate an entity from which or to which the event occurred, and under that rule fall ḍārib, maḍrūb, qātil, maqtūl, and so on. End quote from al-Sharnubi. [Page 236] Definition of compound expression: because it is (if a part of it is intended). Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'and the coined, if intended... etc.' He proceeded here accordingly, and you are aware that there is no need to consider intention here after considering it in the principle of indication. For if you say: Where was it considered in the principle of indication? I say: in his statement: (even if by estimation as we explained). (His statement: 'for the meaning' is attached to 'the coined'. (His statement: 'by correspondence' – the bā' is for association, i.e., a coinage associated with correspondence or meaning, so that it indicates by correspondence. (His statement: 'if a part of it is intended' i.e., the part arranged in hearing. Thus excludes something like 'ḍaraba' because it does not have such a part, since there is no arrangement between the root and the pattern because they are heard simultaneously. So the verb alone is not of the category of compound, but of the category of simple, even if it has two parts: one intended to indicate the event, and the other intended to indicate the time. Al-'Attar: Clarification. End quote. A summary from al-Sayyid and 'Abd al-Ḥakīm with an addition: And when it is clear that these forms are excluded from the divided, they do not fall under either division, even if it is correct that they fall under the simple division by considering each part individually, but the discussion here is about the total of the two words; ponder. (His statement: 'if intended') – in al-Jalāl, there is no need to consider intention here after considering it in the principle of indication, and therefore the Shaykh said: it is needed for understanding, not for completion, end quote. And the author said in the commentary on the original: if by intention is meant actual intention, then compounds before their use and intention of their meanings enter into the definition of simple and exit from the definition of compound. But if by it is meant that it is such that its parts are intended to indicate part of the meaning, then it is compound; otherwise it is simple. So like 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq' as a proper noun, it exits from the definition of simple and enters the definition of compound, because it is such that its two parts are intended to indicate the concept of animal and rational, which are two parts of the person named by it, when applied to a human. And whatever the case, the two definitions are violated both in inclusion and exclusion. End quote. And 'Abd al-Ḥakīm answered that the word is only subject to composition...
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn that divides coined words into compound and simple. The matn states: 'The coined word for the meaning by correspondence is either compound or simple.' The commentator (al-Dasuqi) explains that the matn restricts to 'word' rather than 'signifier' because it is the word that is described as compound or simple, and the discussion is about the indication of words. He clarifies that 'subject' (mawḍū') means either personal coinage (like proper nouns) or generic coinage (like derived terms and metaphors). Al-'Attar then expands on the meaning of 'coined for the meaning'. He explains that coinage can be of three types: (1) the very word for the very meaning (personal or generic), (2) parts for parts (as in compound expressions like 'rāmī al-ḥijārah' where each part has its own meaning), and (3) generic coinage of the compound by form (which is not relevant to the division into compound/simple because that division depends on the parts being arranged in hearing). He then lists what is excluded from the division: non-verbal coinages, meaningless words, combinations of meaningful and meaningless words (which are not truly compound), synonyms (because they do not have parts indicating different meanings), and apposition/emphasis (because they lack composition in the sense of parts contributing to a single meaning). A footnote from al-Sharnubi clarifies the difference between personal and generic coinage. On page 236, the definition of compound is refined: 'if a part of it is intended' meaning the part arranged in hearing. This excludes verbs like 'ḍaraba' because its root and pattern are heard simultaneously and are not arranged in time. Even though the verb has two parts (root and pattern), it is considered simple because the parts are not sequential in hearing. The commentator then discusses the issue of intention, noting that some scholars argue that intention is already considered in the original definition of indication, so it is redundant here. Others differentiate between actual intention and potential intention; if a compound like 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq' used as a proper noun is considered compound because its parts are intended to indicate parts of the meaning when applied to a human. The passage ends with an objection and answer by 'Abd al-Ḥakīm, though it is cut off.

Key Takeaway
The compound word must satisfy both that its parts are intended to indicate part of the meaning and that those parts are sequentially heard.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
10 min
Definition of Compound Expression with Condition of Intent
Commentators refine the definition of compound lafz by adding the condition of intended sequential parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions of intent and sequential hearing to classify an utterance as murakkab or mufrad.

Core Idea
A compound expression requires parts intended sequentially, following the law of placement.

The definition of compound expression (al-murakkab) is not as simple as having multiple parts. Commentators like al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar added crucial conditions: the parts must be intended to signify part of the meaning, they must be heard in sequence, and the intention must follow the original linguistic convention (wad'). This lesson breaks down these conditions so you can correctly identify compound expressions in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the definiendum 'al-murakkab' in the Arabic passage. Note that the definition is being refined by adding conditions.
2
Extract the definition from the passage
Find the phrase 'إن قصد بجزء منه' and understand that the definition requires a part of the utterance to be intended to indicate a part of the meaning.
3
Apply the sequential hearing condition
Note that the intended part must be sequential in hearing (المترتب في السمع). Test this on the verb 'daraba' – its material and pattern are heard simultaneously, so it fails the condition.
4
Apply the law of placement condition
Understand that the intention must accord with the law of placement (قانون الوضع). Example: 'Zayd' used with the intention that 'zay' means 'head' violates this law, so it is not compound.
5
Test the definition on borderline cases
Consider potential counterexamples like 'al-insān' joined with a meaningless part. The commentators explain such combinations are outside the division because the whole is not conventionally placed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف اللفظ المركب لأنَّه إن قصد بجزء منه (أي المترتب في السمع) فخرج نحو ضرب؛ لأنه ليس له جزء كذلك؛ إذ لا ترتيب بين المادة والهيئة؛ لأنهما مسموعان معاً، فالفعل وحده ليس من قبيل المركب، بل من قبيل المفرد، وإن كان له جزآن؛ أحدهما قصد به الدلالة على الحدث، والآخر قصد به الدلالة على الزمان. [ثم قال الدسوقي:] قوله: إن قصد بجزء منه أي: المترتب في السمع، فخرج نحو ضرب؛ لأنه ليس له جزء كذلك؛ إذ لا ترتيب بين المادة والهيئة؛ لأنهما مسموعان معاً، فالفعل وحده ليس من قبيل المركب، بل من قبيل المفرد، وإن كان له جزآن؛ أحدهما: قصد به الدلالة على الحدث، والآخر قصد به الدلالة على الزمان. [وقال العطار:] (قوله: إن قصد ... إلخ) أي: قصداً جارياً على قانون الوضع، فخرج ما إذا قصد بالزاي من زيدٍ الدلالة على عضو من أعضائه كرأسه؛ فلا يكون مركباً بهذا القصد؛ لأنه مخالف لقانون الوضع. ثم قال العطار: والمراد القصد الجاري على قانون الوضع كما صرح به شارح المطالع، فلا يرد أن نحو زيد إذا قصد بجزء منه الدلالة على جزء معناه؛ لأنه على خلاف قانون الوضع. والمراد بقصد الدلالة أن تعتبر تلك الدلالة في إفادة المعنى المقصود من اللفظ؛ سواء كان المفاد صحيحاً أو باطلاً، فيشمل المركبات البديهي بطلان مدلولها، والمركبات المجازية نحو رمى بدر ا.هـ. (قوله: بجزء منه) إن قلت: هذا يصدق بنحو الإنسان إذا ضم إليه مهمل؛ فالأولى أن يقول: بكل جزء منه؛ لتخرج هذه الصورة، وجوابه أنه خارج عن المقسّم؛ لأن المقسّم اللفظ الدال بالمطابقة، والمجموع ليس بموضوع؛ لا بوضع العين ولا بوضع الأجزاء على ما فصل سابقاً، ثم المراد الأجزاء المترتبة في السمع.
Definition of the compound expression: because it is such that a part of it is intended (i.e., the part that is sequential in hearing). Thus, an example like 'daraba' is excluded because it does not have a part like that, since there is no order between the material and the pattern because they are heard together. So the verb alone is not from the category of compound, but from the category of simple, even though it has two parts: one of them is intended to indicate the event, and the other is intended to indicate the time. [Then al-Dasuqi said:] His saying: 'if a part of it is intended' i.e., the part that is sequential in hearing, thus an example like 'daraba' is excluded because it does not have a part like that, since there is no order between the material and the pattern because they are heard together. So the verb alone is not from the category of compound, but from the category of simple, even though it has two parts: one of them is intended to indicate the event, and the other is intended to indicate the time. [And al-'Attar said:] (His saying: 'if intended...etc.') i.e., an intention that proceeds according to the law of placement (wad'). Thus excluded is when one intends with the 'zay' of 'Zayd' to indicate a part of his body like his head; then it is not compound with this intention because it violates the law of placement. Then al-'Attar said: What is meant is the intention that proceeds according to the law of placement, as stated by the commentator of al-Mutali', so the objection that Zayd, if one intends with a part of it to indicate a part of its meaning, does not arise because it violates the law of placement. What is meant by 'intention of indication' is that that indication is considered in conveying the intended meaning from the utterance, whether the conveyed meaning is true or false, so it includes compounds whose signification is obviously false, and metaphorical compounds like 'ramā bi-badr' etc. (His saying: 'with a part of it') If you say: This applies to something like 'al-insān' when joined with a meaningless part; then it would be better to say: 'with every part of it' to exclude this case. The answer is that it is outside the division because the division is the utterance indicating by mutābaqa, and the whole is not placed, neither by the placement of the word nor by the placement of its parts as detailed previously. Then what is meant is the parts that are sequential in hearing.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of the term 'compound expression' (al-lafz al-murakkab). The commentators al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar are refining the definition from the original matn by adding the condition of intent (qasd) and sequentiality in hearing (al-taratub fi al-sam'). The passage begins by restating the core definition: a compound expression is one of which a part is intended to signify a part of the meaning, specifically the parts that are heard in sequence. This immediately excludes a word like 'daraba' (he hit), because although it has two parts (the root d-r-b and the pattern indicating past tense), these are not heard sequentially—they are simultaneous. So 'daraba' is considered simple (mufrad), not compound. This is explained further by al-Dasuqi, who reiterates the point and clarifies that the verb's two parts are intended to signify different things (event and time) yet the word is still simple due to lack of sequentiality. Then al-'Attar introduces the crucial condition that the intent must be 'in accordance with the law of placement' (qasdan jāriyan 'alā qānūn al-waḍ'). This means that the intention to signify a part of the meaning must follow the original linguistic convention, not an arbitrary or accidental intention. For example, the name 'Zayd' has parts (Zay and d), but if someone were to intend the 'zay' to mean 'head' (like a body part), that would violate the law of placement because the word 'Zayd' was not placed for such a meaning. Thus, 'Zayd' remains simple in normal usage. Al-'Attar further explains that the intention of indication (qasd al-dalāla) is considered as long as it serves to convey the intended meaning, regardless of whether the conveyed meaning is true or false. This allows metaphorical compounds like 'ramā bi-badr' (he shot with a full moon, meaning he looked at me with his beloved's eye) to be included as compounds, because the parts are intended according to the metaphorical convention. The passage ends with a potential objection: if the definition requires 'a part of it', then a simple word like 'insān' (human) when combined with a meaningless syllable might seem to fit. The answer is that such a combination is outside the division because the whole is not a conventional expression (mawḍū') either by its own placement or by placement of its parts. Also, the parts must be sequential in hearing, which further narrows the definition. Throughout, the commentators are careful to ensure that the definition of compound expression is precise and excludes apparent counterexamples like verbs, proper names, and arbitrary combinations.

المركب
القصد
قانون الوضع
المترتب في السمع

Definition of Compound and the Condition of Intentionality
Commentator defines compound word and explains the condition that parts must indicate intended parts of meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define murakkab (compound word) and determine whether a given word qualifies as compound based on the condition that its parts intend parts of the meaning.

Core Idea
A compound word is defined by its parts each indicating a part of the intended meaning, not by mere phonetic divisibility.

In this passage, the commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies the definition of 'murakkab' (compound) in the context of logical terminology. He emphasizes that the word must be intended to indicate part of its meaning through its components, and he defends this definition against potential counterexamples like the verb 'darba' and proper nouns like 'Abd Allah'. Understanding this condition is essential for correctly classifying words as simple or compound in Arabic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition's components
The definition is: 'a word that indicates a part of the intended meaning.' Note the three elements: word, indication, part of meaning, and the condition 'intended'.
2
Examine the counterexample 'darba'
The verb 'darba' (he hit) consists of a root (d-r-b) indicating the event and a pattern (fa'ala) indicating past tense and subject. However, the parts are heard simultaneously and indicate the totality of the meaning, not part to part. Thus, it is not a counterexample.
The commentator argues that the indication is to the whole, not to parts.
3
Analyze the proper noun 'Abd Allah'
As a proper noun, the parts 'Abd' and 'Allah' are not intended to indicate parts of the meaning (servanthood and God) but rather denote a specific individual. Thus, it is excluded by the condition of intentionality.
The condition 'al-maqsud' (the intended) is crucial here.
4
Assess the debate on the necessity of the condition
Al-Safawi argues that the condition is unnecessary because the proper noun can be excluded by considering the aspect (haythiyya): from one aspect it is simple (proper meaning) and from another it is compound (literal meaning). The commentator suggests that the condition may be redundant but still useful.
This shows the refinement in logical discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قَوْلُهُ: المَقْصُوْدِ) هذا القيد أشعرَ بِهِ كلامُ المصنّفِ، إِذْ يلزمُ مِنْ كَوْنِ اللَّفْظِ قُصِدَ بِهِ المعنى؛ أنْ يكونَ المعنى مقصوداً، وفي المحشّي أَنَّهُ مَأخوذُ مِن تعريف المعنى ومخرج لمثل عبدِ اللهِ علماً، ونُقِلَ عَنِ السَّيِّدِ عيسَى الصَّفويِّ أَنَّهُ لَا حاجةً إليه في إخراجه؛ لأنَّه بالنَّظر إلى معناه العلمي لم يقصد بجزء منه الدَّلالة على جزء المعنى، فخرج بالقصد الأوَّلِ، وإن صدق عليه أنَّه قصد بجزئه الدَّلالة على جزء المعنى العلمي؛ فهو مُفردٌ ومُركَّبُ مِن جهتين، وذلك لازم مع وجودِ ذلك القيدِ أيضاً ، فلا حاجة إليه، ومُحصِّلُ هذا الاعتماد على قيد الحيثية ا.هـ.
i.e. from: the word (the indication of part of the meaning) intended; (so it is compound), and it is: al-Dasuqi (His saying: the intended) he took it from the definition of meaning; i.e., the intention of the word for it. By al-Attar: that one of the two parts is heard before the other, so the definition is not refuted by something like 'darba'; for it indicates, by considering its material part, the event, and the formal part, the time and relation; indeed the two parts are heard together, but you may say: the intended from something like 'darba' is the indication of the matter and form to the totality of the meaning, not the indication of the part to the part, so no refutation. And what is said that the restriction that the parts are successive in hearing has no evidence for it, is rebutted by the fact that what is understood from the word having parts is that they are heard in reality, i.e., each part of them is heard, not that they are heard together; reflect. (His saying: the intended) this restriction is indicated by the author's statement, since it follows from the word being intended with its meaning that the meaning is intended. And in al-Muhashshi, it is taken from the definition of meaning and excludes something like 'Abd Allah' as a proper noun. And it is transmitted from al-Sayyid 'Isa al-Safawi that there is no need for it in its exclusion; because from the perspective of its proper meaning, no part of it is intended to indicate a part of the meaning, so it is excluded by the first intention. And if it is true that it is intended by its part to indicate a part of the proper meaning, then it is simple and compound from two aspects, and that is necessary even with the existence of that restriction, so there is no need for it. The outcome of this is reliance on the restriction of aspect (al-haythiyya). End.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of compound word (murakkab). The commentator defines compound as a word that indicates a part of the intended meaning. The condition 'al-maqsud' (the intended) is crucial to exclude cases like 'darba' (he hit) where the parts (root and pattern) are heard simultaneously and can be argued to indicate the whole meaning, not part to part. The commentator also discusses the example of 'Abd Allah' as a proper noun. The objection from al-Safawi is that the condition is unnecessary because the proper noun is not intended part-wise. The commentator concludes by emphasizing the reliance on the aspect (haythiyya) rather than the condition of qasd. The passage follows the order: first the statement of the definition, then the explanation of the condition, then the defense against potential counterexamples, then further discussion on the necessity of the condition.

Distinguishing Complete and Incomplete Compounds
Defining complete compound with example of subject-predicate necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a complete compound (tam) by applying the criterion of independent intelligibility and differentiate it from an incomplete compound using examples from Arabic grammar and Islamic texts.

Core Idea
A complete compound needs no crutch; an incomplete one always calls for another word.

In this passage, the author introduces a fundamental division of compound expressions into complete (tam) and incomplete (naqis). The key is whether the compound is independently intelligible, meaning it does not necessitate another word to be understood. This distinction is essential for analyzing statements in logic, Arabic grammar, and Islamic legal theory. We will unpack the definition, examine the example given, and apply the criterion to real sentences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining condition
The author says: 'if the complaint upon it is correct, such that it does not necessitate another word.' Focus on the phrase 'لا يكون مستدعياً لفظ آخر' — it must not require another word for its meaning to be clear.
The 'complaint' refers to linguistic scrutiny; the definition is linguistic, not legal.
2
Understand the core necessity
The author gives the example of the subject necessitating the predicate and vice versa. This is the paradigmatic case of incompleteness. In a sentence, the subject (musnad ilayhi) and predicate (musnad) mutually depend on each other. A compound lacking either is incomplete.
For example, 'زيد' alone is not a complete statement; it needs a predicate like 'قائم'.
3
Examine the counterexample
The passage provides: 'ضرب زيد أمس' (Zaid hit yesterday). If you remove 'أمس', the remaining 'ضرب زيد' is a complete compound. This shows that optional qualifiers (like objects, adverbs) are not necessitated — they only add detail, but the core predication is already sufficient.
The verb 'ضرب' with its subject 'زيد' forms the essential predication; 'أمس' is extra.
4
Apply the criterion to new examples
Test the definition: Is 'زيد قائم' complete? Yes, because it has a subject and predicate and does not require another word. Is 'ضرب' alone complete? No, because it lacks a subject. Is 'إن كان زيد قائماً' complete? No, because the conditional 'إن' necessitates a result clause.
Remember: only the subject-predicate core is essential; transitive verbs' objects are optional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قَوْلُهُ : كَاسْتِدْعَاءِ) صفةٌ مصدرٍ محذوفٍ؛ أي : مُستدعياً استدعاء كاستدعاء، وأشار بذلك إلى أنَّ الإسناد يتمُّ بالمسندِ إليه والمسندِ، ولا يُنافي ذلكَ توقُفُ الفعل المتعدي على مفعوله أو قيدهِ كالحالِ مثلاً ، فإنَّ ذلكَ مِمَّا تكثرُ بِهِ الفائدة وتتربّى ولا يتوقَّفُ حصولها عليه، فانتِظَارُ المفعول بهِ أو الحالِ ليس كانتظار المسندِ إليه أو المسنَدِ، ونقل المحشّي هنا عن السَّيِّدِ الصَّفوي كلاماً ادَّعَى البعضُ عدم صحتِهِ، وهو كَمَا قالَ، وَلولا خَوفُ الإطالة؛ لأشبعْنَا فيه المقالة.
Section: On the investigation of words [Divisions of the compound] A. (Either complete) if the complaint upon it is correct, such that it does not necessitate another word, like the necessitation of the predicate for the subject, and vice versa. al-Dasuqi (His saying: if it is correct) i.e., the complaint is approved, and by correctness is meant linguistic correctness, which is approval, not legal correctness which is the opposite of corruption; because the word is not described as correct or lacking correctness. (His saying: such that it is not ... etc.) the ba is for cause or for depiction; i.e., the correctness of the complaint upon it is depicted by that compound word not necessitating ... etc. (His saying: necessitating) i.e., requiring. (His saying: like the necessitation ... etc.) is an example of the negated; i.e., a necessitation like the necessitation ... etc., not like the necessitation of excesses as in: darba Zaidun amsi (Zaid hit yesterday), for if you delete amsi, the speech would be complete. al-Attar: An objection from some marginal commentators that the definition of meaning has no indication of its being intended; it is prevented because intention is a relation; one of its two sides is the meaning, and the other side is the word, since the word is described as being intended by the meaning when the active intention is attached to it, so inevitably that description is obtained for the meaning upon intention, and the definition points to this description; reflect. (His saying: either complete) it is better to say 'either a complete compound' because it is the noun, but they often are lenient in such matters. (His saying: like the necessitation) is a description of a deleted infinitive; i.e., necessitating a necessitation like the necessitation, and by that he indicated that the predication is completed by the subject and the predicate, and that does not contradict the dependence of the transitive verb on its object or its qualifier like the state, for example, for that is something by which the benefit abounds and increases, and its attainment does not depend on it, so waiting for the object or the state is not like waiting for the subject or the predicate. And al-Muhashshi transmitted here from al-Sayyid al-Safawi a statement that some claimed is not correct, and it is as he said, and were it not for fear of prolongation, we would have elaborated on it fully.

Commentary
This passage introduces the division of compound words into complete (tam) and incomplete (naqis). The complete compound is defined as one that is independently intelligible, not requiring another word to complete its meaning, like the need of a predicate for a subject. The commentary explains the phrase 'if the complaint upon it is correct' as linguistic appropriateness. The example 'darba Zaidun amsi' shows that if you remove 'amsi', the speech stands complete, illustrating the distinction between necessary dependence (like subject-predicate) and optional elaboration (like objects and adverbs). The passage then includes an objection by some marginal commentators about the definition of meaning lacking indication of intention, which is rebutted by explaining that intention is a relation between word and meaning. Finally, a grammatical note prefers to say 'complete compound' rather than 'either complete', and further clarifies that predication is achieved by subject and predicate, while transitive verbs' objects are not essential.

The Complete Composite: Definition and Conditions
Author defines the complete composite and explains its condition of not requiring another word.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a complete composite (tamm) by applying the condition of sihhat al-sukut and distinguish it from incomplete composites.

Core Idea
A composite is complete if silence upon it is valid — it does not require another word for its essential meaning.

We now examine the division of composite expressions into complete and incomplete. The matn defines the complete composite as that upon which silence is valid. This lesson unpacks that definition, clarifying what 'sihhat al-sukut' means and how it applies to different linguistic structures. The commentary by al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar refines the condition, showing that essential dependence (like predicate on subject) differs from accidental dependence (like transitive verb on object). Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing logical propositions and grammatical sentences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Definiendum
The term being defined is 'tamm' (complete), which refers to a composite expression. In the matn, it is the first division of murakkab. Note that the commentator recommends saying 'murakkab tamm' rather than just 'tamm'.
2
Extract the Genus and Differentia
The genus is 'composite expression' (murakkab). The differentia is 'sihhat al-sukut' (validity of silence), explained as the composite not requiring another word for its meaning. The phrase 'bi-an la yakuna mustad'iyan' gives the condition explicitly.
The ba' in 'bihalla yakuna' is for exemplification or depiction.
3
Understand the Condition: Essential vs. Accidental Dependence
The commentary clarifies that 'mustad'i' (requiring) here means essential need, like the need of a predicate for a subject (isnad). A transitive verb's need for an object or a restriction like a time phrase is not essential; it increases benefit but completion of predication does not depend on it. Example: 'Daraba Zaidun ams' – if you remove 'ams', the utterance remains complete because the verb-subject pair is sufficient.
Al-Dasuqi distinguishes between waiting for the subject/predicate (essential) and waiting for the object/state (accidental).
4
Apply the Definition to Examples
Test candidate expressions: 'Zaidun qa'imun' (complete), 'Daraba Zaidun' (complete despite missing object), 'In kana Zaidun' (incomplete, needs apodosis). The condition of sihhat al-sukut requires that one can stop without needing further words for basic intelligibility.
The commentary by al-'Attar addresses an objection about meaning and intention, reinforcing that the definition points to the intended meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
